Summary 33:
Gross national product (GNI) and gross domestic product (GDP) data
Gross domestic product data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 549/2013 on the harmonisation of national accounts
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a procedure to verify and assess the comparability, reliability and exhaustiveness of Gross National Product (GNP) data and GNI data within the European System of Accounts (ESA 2010).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries and to the European Commission (Eurostat).
It establishes a procedure for the verification and assessment of the quality of the national accounts data and the data for the gross national product and gross national income (GND) components of the European system of accounts.
Delegated acts
The delegated acts are adopted by the Commission for a period of 5 years from 18 April 2019.
The Commission has delegated the power to adopt delegated acts to the EU countries, the European Parliament and the Council for the purpose of verifying and assessing the quality and reliability of the GNI and GND data.
EU countries’ experts are assisted by the European Social and Social Committee (ESSC).
The ESSC assists the Commission in the preparation of delegated acts and assists it in the verification of the data it receives from the national statistical authorities of EU countries.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
The legislation entered into force on 18 April 2013.
It has applied since 18 April 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s’ national statistical office, Eurostat, is responsible for the collection, transmission and dissemination of national statistics.
For more information, see:
‘Gross National Product’ and ‘GDP’ on the Eurostat website.
* KEY TERMS
* Gross national product: the final result of the production of goods and services by resident institutional units (final consumption and gross capital formation) plus exports and minus imports of goods or services;
GDP: the sum of uses in the total economy generation of income account (compensation of employees, taxes on production and imports less subsidies, gross operating surplus and mixed income of the whole economy).
* GNI: the aggregate value of gross national products (GNCs) and the components of GNI aggregates and their components, in accordance with the definitions referred to in Article 1.1.1 of the regulation (see summary).

Summary 34:
EU type-approval of vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units — EU-wide rules for vehicles, components, separate technical unit and separate electrical units
EU type approval of vehicles and systems, parts, separate electronic units and separate electronic components
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 168/2013 on the granting of EU type approval for certain types of vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedures for granting EU type approvals for certain vehicles and components that are already approved in the United Kingdom (‘UK’) and that have been placed on the EU’s market.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
vehicles, systems*, components* and separate mechanical units* that have previously been approved by the UK type-authorisation authority;
separate electrical units* which have been previously approved by a manufacturer in the UK.
It does not apply to systems* or separate electrical unit* that are new to the EU market or that have not yet been certified in the EU.
Vehicle manufacturers may apply on a voluntary basis for a European Union type approval on the basis of certain requirements applicable to new types of systems* and components* for new vehicles.
The EU typeapproval is granted by the European Commission or the competent national authority of the EU country in which the vehicle manufacturer is located.
Manufacturers may also apply for an EU type of approval for a vehicle that has been approved in another EU country on the same type.
If a vehicle manufacturer does not have a UK type approval, the vehicle type may be placed on their EU market if it has been certified by a national authority in the same EU country.
A vehicle manufacturer may apply for a EU type* approval of a vehicle type that has previously been certified or certified in a country other than the UK by the national authority responsible for the certification of vehicles in that EU country, if the type of vehicle remains otherwise identical to the one approved in Britain.
Application for a type* may only be made by manufacturers established in an EU country that is not a member of the European Economic Area (EEA).
The vehicle manufacturer must submit a request to the national competent authority of a non-EU country for the type to be approved.
This request must contain the following information:
the vehicle type;
the date on which the type was certified in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 167/2013 (see summary);
the type of system, component or separate electronic unit that the manufacturer has requested the EU type to approve;
a type of separate electrical or electronic unit which the manufacturer is responsible for certifying.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Directive 2007/46/EC lays down the rules for the granting and granting of type approvals by national authorities in the EEA, the EU and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA).
It has applied since 30 March 2019 and had to become law in the Member States by 30 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to ensure the smooth functioning of the single market in the event of the withdrawal of the UK from the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘EU type approvals’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Vehicle: any vehicle, system or separate component that is a component of a motor vehicle and that has had its type approved by an EEA or EFTA authority.

Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 169/2013 of 30 March 2013 on the grant of EU types of vehicle and components and of separate electronic and electrical units and repealing Regulation (EEC, EFTA, EEA and EEA/EEA/EFTA (OJ L 347, 30.4.2013, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (U 169/13/EC have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 35:
European Police Office (Europol) — rules on the use of personal data and the processing of information by Europol
European Police Organisation (EuroPOL) — ways of using personal data
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2009/371/JHA establishing a European Police Office
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
 
   
It establishes Europol, the European Union’s (EU) law enforcement agency, which is responsible for preventing, investigating and prosecuting crime in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision sets out the rules for the operation of Europol.
Europol is the main law enforcement body for the EU and the EU countries’ police forces.
It is the EU law enforcement arm of Interpol, a global police organisation based in the United Nations (UN).
It works closely with Interpol in order to prevent, investigate and prosecute organised crime and other forms of organised crime, as well as to support and strengthen actions and cooperation in tackling forms of crime that affect the interests of the EU or its Member States.
Its main tasks are to protect security and public order or to prevent crime, to guarantee that a national investigation will not be jeopardised or to protect the rights and freedoms of third parties.
The regulation sets out rules on how Europol uses personal data to fulfil its tasks.
EU countries may refuse or restrict access to their nationals’ personal data if, with due regard to their interests, it is necessary to fulfil their tasks properly, to protect their security or public order.
to protect personal data against accidental or unlawful destruction, accidental loss or loss or unauthorised disclosure, alteration and access or any other form of processing of their personal data.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The European police office was set up under Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union, on the establishment of a European Union police office.
For more information, see:
‘Europol’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Personal data: information on a person or a group of persons, such as their name, address, date of birth, sex, gender, nationality, place of residence, nationality and place of employment.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Decisions 2009/391/JHHA of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2009 establishing a Europol and repealing Decision 2009/373/Jha of the Court of Justice of the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy (JHA) (OJ C 202, 12.12.2009, pp. 1-8)
DecISION 2010/391-JHA of 11 January 2010 repealing Decision 2010/371-JH)
See consolidated version.

Summary 36:
European Instrument for temporary support to mitigate unemployment risks in an emergency (SURE) following the COVID-19 outbreak (2018/1046)
European Intervention Instrument for Temporary Assistance to Mitigate Unemployment Risks in an Emergency (SURES)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1047 — European Instrument for short-term support to alleviate unemployment risks following COVID‐19 outbreak
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets up a European instrument for temporary assistance to mitigate the impact of COVID–19 on the labour market in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation provides for financial assistance by the EU to EU countries that are in difficulties or are seriously threatened with severe difficulties caused by exceptional occurrences beyond their control.
EU countries can request financial assistance from the EU in the form of a loan or a guarantee.
The EU countries must provide the EU with:
the amount of the loan, the maximum average maturity, its pricing formula, its maximum number of instalments, its availability period and the other detailed rules needed for the granting of the financial assistance;
an assessment of the compliance by the country with the conditions set out in Article 3;
a description of the national short-time work schemes or similar measures, as well as, where appropriate, of the relevant health-related measures that may be financed.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 8 May 2018.
BACKGROUND
The COVID outbreak caused societal disruptions and increased public expenditure in a growing number of EU countries.
For more information, see:
‘SURE’ on the European Commission’s website.
KEY TERMS
* Temporary assistance: the EU provides financial assistance to an EU country that is in difficulty or is seriously threatened to be in difficulties due to an exceptional occurrence beyond its control, such as an outbreak of a contagious disease.
* EU country: any EU country which has been affected by a communicable disease outbreak.

Summary 39:
The European Security and Defence College (ESDC)
The European Strategic and Defence Centre (ESSC)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2016/2382 — setting up the European Strategic & Defence College
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE REGULATION?
The ESDC is a network bringing together civilian and military institutes, colleges, academies, universities, institutions, centres of excellence and other actors dealing with security and defence policy issues within the EU as identified by EU countries, as well as the European Union Institute for Security Studies (EUISS).
The decision sets up the ESDC to provide training and education in the field of the EU’s Common Security and Defense Policy (CFDP) in the wider context of the Common Foreign and Security Policy at European level to develop and promote a common understanding of CFSP and CSDP, to identify and disseminate best practices in relation to various CFSP issues, and to support the development of research and other activities related to CFSP.
KEY POINTS
   
The purpose of the decision and the regulation is to establish a network of European institutions and institutes working together on CFSP-related issues.
The establishment of the network is part of the European Defence and Security Council (EDC) and the European External Action Service (EAS) and is a key element of the common foreign and security policy.
Eligibility for funding
The ESDC is open to all EU Member States and non-EU countries.
It is funded by the EU budget.
Member States may put forward candidates for the position of Head of the EDSC, who is appointed by the EAS and is assisted by a Steering Committee composed of representatives of the Member States, EU countries and EU institutions.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Decision entered into force on 23 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
The ESCDC was set up by Council Joint Action 2008/550/CFSP, which, in turn, was replaced by Council Decision 2013/189/CFDPSP (3).
For more information, see:
European Defence and Strategic and Security College (EADSC) (European Commission).

Summary 40:
Reclaimed water — water quality and monitoring — minimum requirements for water use in the EU (Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 and Regulation (EU, 2019/852)
Reclaimed waters — minimum provisions for water quality, monitoring and distribution
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) 2018/1101 — minimum water quality requirements for the use of reclaimed water
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets minimum requirements to be met by EU countries in order to ensure that water used for agriculture, forestry and aquaculture can be reused for other purposes.
It aims to reduce the strain on the EU’s water resources.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
reclamation facilities* for the production and supply of water;
recovery of water from aquifers used for farming and forestry;
the reclamation of water used to irrigate crops, pastures and forage, fibre, ornamental, seed, energy and turf crops;
drinking water for human consumption;
water used for drinking, bathing, cooking, washing, washing and other domestic purposes;
seawater used for the treatment of agricultural products.
EU countries must ensure that:
the parties responsible for the drawing up of the water reuse risk management plan and the competent authority for the granting of the permit for production or supply of the reclaimed water have no conflict of interests;
there are no conflicts of interest between the parties.
The water must be treated in a way that does not harm the environment or human or animal health.
the water must not be exposed to animals or plants.
Reclamation facilities may be exempted from the requirements of the regulation if the following criteria are met:
they are not located within a water body used for water abstraction or a relevant safeguard zone designated pursuant to Directive 2000/60/EC;
they do not have to be located in an area where the water is not intended for human use or a safeguard zone;
their activities do not affect the water resources of the EU or the environment of EU countries.
In addition, the regulation provides for the exemption of research or pilot projects in relation to reclamation facilities from the water quality or monitoring requirements.
REFERENCES

Summary 41:
EU blue card for non-EU nationals — rules for granting and renewing their EU blue card (European Union (EU) 2019/2021)
EU blue car for non - EU nationals
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2017/22/EU on the European Union blue card
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out rules for the granting and renewal of EU blue cards (EU blue cards) to third-country nationals who are not nationals of an EU country, EU citizens or citizens of another country in the European Economic Area (EEA).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to nationals of any EU country who have a bachelor’s degree or a professional qualification and who have lived or worked in the EU for at least 3 years.
It does not apply to people who have not obtained a degree or have not worked for more than 3 years or who do not have professional experience.
EU countries must ensure that applicants meet the following criteria:
have at least 2 years’ professional experience in the relevant EU country;
have a minimum salary threshold of at least €10,000 for each of the 3 years of required professional experience;
be able to provide for a lower salary threshold for the period following their graduation.
Applicants must submit a complete application to the EU country concerned within 30 days of submitting the application for an initial or renewal of a blue card.
The EU country that receives the application must verify that the applicant meets all the criteria for admission and that the person concerned has the necessary qualifications.
has the right to move to a second EU country or to live and work in another EU country.
An EU country may reject an application for admission if:
the documents presented have been fraudulently acquired, falsified or tampered with;
the person concerned is considered to pose a threat to public policy, public security or public health; or
the employer's business was established or operates for the main purpose of facilitating the entry of non-EEA nationals.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
  
The European Commission has published a communication on the implementation of the directive.
For more information, see:
‘The EU Blue Card’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Non-EU country: a country where nationals of one EU country are not allowed to enter its territory for the purpose of business activities or to reside and work there for the purposes of mobility.
* Third-country national: a person who is not an EU citizen, EU citizen or EU citizen of an EEA country, but who is a resident of a country other than that of the EU.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2017/20/EU of 21 December 2017 on the application of the European Community blue card to third countries for the benefit of third countries (European Commission)

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s migration policy for 2019-2021.
It aims to support EU countries’ efforts to cope with the influx of displaced persons and refugees.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EMF supports EU countries in their efforts to deal with the migration crisis by providing financial support for actions in or in relation to non-EU countries.
EU countries are required to:
provide the European Commission with information, communication and publicity on their actions;
ensure that their actions have clear EU added value;
promote a balance of efforts between countries in receiving such persons and bearing the consequences thereof;
support the integration of refugees and migrants in their communities;
facilitate the transfer of asylum seekers to another EU country;
encourage the exchange of information, skills and information between EU countries;
develop the capacity of EU countries to manage their borders and their internal borders;
contribute to border management.
Funding
The regulation sets out how the EMF is funded and how it is managed.
The European Commission is responsible for managing the EU migration fund. It is assisted by the European Border and Coast Guard and the European Maritime Safety Agency.
the European Migration Network.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPACT APPLY?
Regulation (EU/2020/2021/2022 has applied since 15 October 2019 and had to become law in the EU countries by 15 October 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EUMF is part of the common EU migration policy.
For more information, see:
EU migration policy (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission Implementing Decision (EU, Euratom) 2020/1897 of 15 October 2020 establishing the European Union Migration fund (OJ L 183, 15.10.2020, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) 2019–2022 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 43:
Family-related leave — equal treatment between men and women
Family - related leave — equitable treatment between women and men
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2010/18/EU on family leave
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure equal treatment of mothers and fathers taking parental leave in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that all workers have the right to take parental leave, irrespective of whether they are men or women.
The directive applies to all EU workers, regardless of their gender.
It does not apply to workers who are employed by a small or medium-sized enterprise (SME).
It also applies to employees who work for a medium or large enterprise (i.e. a company with fewer than 500 employees) and to employees working for a company that has more than 1,000 employees.
This directive does not affect the rights of employees who are covered by collective agreements or collective agreements with other EU countries, such as the European Social Fund (ESF) or the European Investment Bank (ESB).
In addition, EU countries may require prior medical certification of the need for their care policies, including with regard to carers’ leave.
Employers may also postpone the granting of parental leave for a reasonable period of time on the grounds that the taking of the leave would seriously disrupt the good functioning of the employer’s business or would seriously interfere with its good functioning.
In the case of postponement, the employer must provide reasons for such a postponement in writing in writing.
If an employer is not able to provide the reasons for postponing the granting the leave, it must inform the European Commission of the reason for the postponement and inform the Commission of its decision.
Workers may request that their employer provide them with flexible working arrangements for the duration of their parental leave. These arrangements may include:
a payment or an allowance corresponding to the last two weeks of the parental leave as provided for in Article 8(3) of the directive;
the use of that payment or allowance in order to guarantee the effective take-up of the right by carers, in particular by men.
Flexible working arrangements
The Directive requires EU countries to ensure that workers are entitled to:
take parental leave on a flexible basis;
have access to childcare and long-term care services;
be able to work from home;
take part of their leave in a flexible way.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 2 August 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 2 August 2024.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Family-Related Leave’ on the European Council's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
  ‘Family leave directive’ of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 June 2010 on family-related leaves (OJ L 183, 27.6.2010, pp. 1-8)

Summary 44:
Sanctions in the area of fraud affecting the EU’s financial interests — definition and application
Sanctions on fraud affecting its financial interests – definition and implementation
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/852 on the definition of criminal offences and sanctions in the areas of fraud and misappropriation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out minimum rules for the imposition of sanctions and the application of criminal penalties in the EU in order to protect the EU's financial interests from fraud.
It aims to prevent and combat fraud that could seriously damage the European Union's (EU's) financial interests.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to non-EU countries, such as the United Kingdom (1) and Ireland (1), which are not party to the European Convention on the Protection of the European Communities’ Financial Interests (the ‘Convention’).
It applies to public officials, natural persons, public bodies, offices and agencies, as well as to private persons.
EU countries must define the offences covered by the directive.
The definition of ‘fraud’ and ‘misappropriation’ means the action of a public official who is directly or indirectly entrusted with the management of funds or assets to commit or disburse funds or appropriate or use assets contrary to the purpose for which they were intended in any way which damages or is likely to damage the Union's financial interest.
’Misappropriation is defined as a criminal offence if it is intentionally committed by a person who:
is in the exercise of his or her functions in a way which seriously damages or affects the EU´s’ financial interests;
is committed intentionally by an individual or group of persons, including a company or organisation;
does not result from an act or omission that is objectively, objectively, factual or has a material effect on the intended purpose and whereby the EU�s financial interest are damaged.
Penalties
The maximum level of sanctions should not go beyond what is proportionate for the offences. However, EU countries may provide for sanctions other than criminal sanctions.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYEARS OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 6 July 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 6 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
 ‘Fraud affecting the Union’ is one of the most serious forms of fraud that can harm the EU. It can result from the misuse of EU funds or from the misapplication of public funds.
For more information, see:
‘Financial interests’ on the European Commission's website.

Scope

They must be able to lodge complaints with the competent authorities of their EU country and have the right to have them settled out of court if they believe that they have not been treated fairly or that way by the EU country.

Summary 47:
Inland waterways: statistics on passenger transport
Inlandlocked inland waterways
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on statistics on inland waterways and repealing Council Directive 80/1119/EEC
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the transmission of data on passenger and freight transport by inland waterways in the European Union (EU).
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 1365/2006 on statistics of goods transport by waterways.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the transport of goods and freight across the EU’s inland waterways, including by cross-border transport services.
It does not apply to passenger transport.
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on:
total tonnages transported by vessels;
total movements of goods transported;
traffic in containers and empty containers;
loadings of vessels; and
movements of vessels used solely for non-commercial purposes by port administrations and public authorities.
The Commission must transmit the data to Eurostat in accordance with the national statistical institutes and other national authorities of EU countries.
National authorities must supply the Commission with the following information:
the number of tonnes transported by vessel;
the total number of tonnes transported by vehicle;
transport of goods in containers, empty containers and containers not in containers or empty containers.
In addition, EU countries must supply Eurostat with information on the number of containers transported and movements of vessels.
the type of vessel, the vessel and the type of container or container or empty container.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRYING REGULATIONS APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
In 2014, the Commission adopted Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 966/2012, which aims to reduce energy consumption and the environmental impact of transport by improving the efficiency of EU transport networks.
For more information, see:
‘Inland water transport’ on Eurostat's website.

Delegated Regulation on the suspension mechanism for the exemption from the Schengen visa requirement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

Scope

the European Economic Area (EEA);
the Republic of Ireland, Iceland, Liechtenstein and Norway;
Switzerland;
Norway, Iceland and Norway.
The suspension mechanism
Annex II applies to third countries in respect of which the situation has changed as regards those criteria and which are no longer covered by Annex I.
A delegated act may be adopted by the European Commission to suspend the application of Annex II for a period of 18 months for all nationals of the third country concerned.
subjecting a third country to a suspension of its visa requirement must be notified to the European Parliament, the Council or the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC).
The Commission must adopt a delegated act in accordance with the procedure laid down in Regulation (EC) No 182/2011/2011. The Commission is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of the EU countries and the EEC countries and chaired by the Commission’s Vice-President for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy.

Summary 50:
Eurojust — legal framework for the European Union’s external action unit (Eurojust) (European External Action Service) (Eurostat)
Eurojust is the EU body responsible for investigating and prosecuting war crimes and crimes against humanity.
ACT
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 February 2014 on the legal framework of Eurojust
SUMMARY
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up Eurojust, a European Union body that investigates and prosecutes war crimes.
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ national authorities have the necessary legal and operational powers to effectively carry out their missions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except the United Kingdom (1) and the United States of America (1).
It does not apply to non-EU countries.
EU countries are responsible for:
collecting, processing and storing operational personal data for Eurojust;
providing Eurojust with operational data;
assisting Eurojust in its work;
coordinating with other EU bodies, offices and agencies, such as the European Commission, the Court of Auditors, the European Court of Justice and the European Judicial Network (OLAF).
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation.
The EU countries have the power to:
issue or execute any request for mutual legal assistance or mutual recognition;
order, request or execute investigative measures, as provided for in Directive 2014/41/EU on criminal law;
implement investigative measures;
inform Eurojust of their national laws and procedures;
make requests for judicial assistance or judicial assistance;
approve or refuse to grant judicial assistance.
National authorities must inform Eurojust if they have concluded a cooperation agreement or working arrangement with another EU body, office or agency.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION APPLY?
Decision 2014/552/EU has applied since 1 March 2014.
BACKGROUND
  
The Eurojust mission is to investigate and prosecute war crimes, crimes of aggression and crimes of war, as well as trafficking in human beings and child sexual exploitation.
For more information, see:
‘Eurojust’ on the Eurojust website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 51:
Combating money laundering by means of criminal law (Directive (EU) 2019/849 — combating money laundering (criminal law)
Combating the money laundering problem (criminal laws)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2017/19/EU — combatting money laundering
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to combat the problem of money laundering in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU countries.
It does not apply to non-EU countries, such as the United Kingdom (1) and the United States (1).
EU countries are required to criminalise money laundering when it is committed by persons who have committed, or were involved in, the criminal activity from which the proceeds of the property was derived.
Penalties
The legislation applies to:
money laundering committed recklessly or by serious negligence;
the commission of any offence punishable by deprivation of liberty or a detention order for a maximum of more than one year or, as regards EU countries that have a minimum threshold for offences in their legal systems, for a minimum of 6 months;
any offence punishable, in accordance with national law, by deprivation or detention for more than six months.
The law also applies to money laundering committed by natural persons acting as obliged entities in the exercise of their professional activities.
In criminal proceedings regarding money laundering, EU countries must assist each other in the widest possible way and ensure that information is exchanged in an effective and timely manner.
National authorities must establish their jurisdiction in order to enable the competent authorities to investigate and prosecute such activities and to exchange information.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYORKREFERENCES

Summary 52:
EU bluefin tuna fishery — rules for fishing
EU Bluefin tuna fisheries — rules on fishing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the management of the EU’s Bluefin Tuna fishery
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for managing the EU bluefin fish fishery in the eastern Atlantic and the Mediterranean.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU fishing vessels fishing in the Atlantic Ocean, the Mediterranean and the Black Sea.
It does not apply to the fishing of Atlantic tuna for sport and recreational purposes.
EU fishing vessels must comply with the following rules:
fishing vessels must have an individual quota for the bluefin species;
fisheries must be managed according to the International Convention for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (ICCAT) rules;
the catch must be kept on board and accessible at any time for control purposes;
management measures must be in line with the rules laid down in the Convention on the International Conservation of Tropical Tunas and the International Fisheries Convention (CITC) (see summary).
EU countries must ensure that:
the vessels are accompanied by a national of an EU country responsible for the fishery;
there is a national observer programme in place to monitor and monitor the fishing activities of EU vessels;
EU vessels must be accompanied by an ICCAT record of all fishing vessels authorised to fish in the EU fishing fleet.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION APPLY?
the regulation has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
It applies from 1 July 2012.
For more information, see:
‘Bluefin tuna’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Bluefin fish: a species of tuna that is the subject of an international conservation agreement.

Summary 53:
Enforcement of injurious pricing charges in the EU maritime industry — common rules for shipbuilding and related activities (codified Regulation (EU) No 182/2011)
En enforcement of infringements in the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 652/2011 — common procedures for enforcing injurious prices in the maritime industry
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down common rules to ensure that shipbuilding companies who sell a vessel to a non-EU country at less than normal value can be held liable for any damage caused by the sale.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 181/2011.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the sale of a vessel by a shipbuilder to an exporting country. It does not apply to the purchase of a similar vessel by an EU country.
EU countries must notify the European Commission if they believe that the sale is causing or has caused injury.
The Commission may decide to initiate an investigation if it considers that there is sufficient evidence to justify such an action.
An investigation must be opened within 45 days of the date on which the complaint was lodged, or, in the case of initiation, no later than 6 months from the time the sale was known or should have been known, and must publish a notice in the Official Journal of the European Union (OJ).
The investigation may be terminated if the shipbuilder does not pay the charge within 1 year from the date of initiation of the investigation.
Investigations may be opened by the Commission acting in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 10(2 of the regulation.
If the Commission considers that an investigation is necessary, it must inform the exporting country of the reason for initiating the investigation and the reasons for the rejection of the complaint or the initiation of proceedings.
In the event of a decision to open an investigation, the Commission must provide information to the EU countries and the EU shipbuilding industry, including:
the shipbuilder’s name, address and contact details;
the country of origin of the vessel;
details of the type and quantity of the ship and the type of vessel to be sold;
any other information that may be provided by the shipbuilding company;
information provided by interested parties to the Commission;
a list of factors that may affect the calculation of the price of the sale, including the volume and value of EU production of the like vessel and the number of vessels in service.
Implementation of the regulations
The EU countries must ensure that the Commission adopts the measures necessary for the implementation of the procedures laid down in the regulation, including all measures necessary to take countermeasures.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Decision (EU), Euratom — Common procedures for enforcement in the Maritime Industry (Codified Regulation(EU) 2018/852)
   
Directive 2014/34/EU — Common rules for the enforcement of the injurious-pricing-charges in the marine industry (Codification of Regulation (Euratom) (Official Journal C 202/2014)
Directives 2014/35/EU, 2014/36/EU and 2014/37/EU have applied since 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to ensure the smooth functioning of the maritime market, in particular by harmonising the rules and procedures for the application of the legislation.
For more information, see:
‘Injunctive pricing charges’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Injurious pricing: a price which is calculated to result in a profit which is not a reasonable one at the time of sale, even if the sale price was calculated at a price that was reasonable at that time.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision (EU Euratom, Euratom, of 23 June 2011 on common procedures in the area of injudicious pricing in the Marine Industry (codification of Council Regulation (C 202/2011, Official Journal C 204/2014, Official J L 347, 29.6.2011, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Decision (EURatom) 2018 /852 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 54:
Cableway installations and cableway safety components — EU rules for the design, manufacture and installation of cableways
Cableways and cableways safety components – EU rules to ensure the safety of persons and property
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on cableway installations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that cableways are safe for people and property when properly installed, maintained and operated in accordance with their intended purpose.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all cableways in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to cableways that are not covered by Directive 2000/9/EC on the harmonisation of technical harmonisation and standards.
It applies to the design and manufacture of cables and safety components for cableways, as well as to the installation and operation of such installations.
EU countries must ensure that:
cableways are designed and constructed in such a way that they do not pose a risk to persons or property;
they are installed and maintained in a manner that ensures their safety;
the components are installed correctly and are operated correctly.
The EU declaration of conformity must accompany each cableway installation or safety component.
Manufacturers and importers must notify the competent authorities of the EU country in which the cableway is located when they place it into service.
Importers must inform EU countries of the type of cableway they intend to place into service and the type and type of the components that they will place in service, and of the cables they will use.
where appropriate, they must inform the EU countries in which they will put it in service and inform them of the design of the cableways and the types of safety components to be installed.
National authorities must notify EU countries if they believe that a cableway does not comply with the requirements laid down in the regulation.
Notified bodies must carry out an assessment, notification or monitoring of the conformity of the subsystem or safety components concerned before affixing the CE marking on them.
They must also carry out a safety analysis and safety report for planned cableways.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLYORK?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
Cables are used to transport goods and passengers across the EU by means of cables that are designed, manufactured and installed in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Cables’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Cableway installations: installations that are intended to provide a means of transport by cableway across the territory of an EU country, such as roads, railways, bridges, tunnels, dams, canals or other types of water, air, land, sea or sea water, and are designed to be used for the transport of goods and persons.

Directive (EU) 2018/1972 on the assessment of accessibility requirements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to increase the availability of accessible products* and services on the EU’s internal market and improve the accessibility of relevant information.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
products and services that are sold on the internal market in the EU;
services that are provided by economic operators, such as transport services and regional transport services.
It does not apply to microenterprises providing services within the scope of this directive.
EU countries must ensure that:
the accessibility of the products and the services covered by the directive is met;

electronic ticketing services;
rail passengers’ tickets;
accessibility of information on vehicles, the surrounding infrastructure and the built environment and on accessibility of buildings;
contact information on the information of persons with disability;
computer assistance for persons using assistive technologies, in accessible modes of communication.
Disputes between EU countries are handled by the European Commission and EU countries’ competent national authorities.

Summary 56:
Supply chain due diligence of EU importers of minerals and metals — compliance with the OECD’s due diligence guidelines
Suppliers of metals and minerals must comply with the guidelines of the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD) and the United Nations (UN).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2011 — supply chain due-diligence obligations of European Union importers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU imports and exports of minerals, metals and concentrates from conflict-affected areas in the African Great Lakes region and the Horn of Africa are subject to due diligence procedures.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all importers who import and process minerals or metals into the EU.
It does not apply to companies that are involved in the illegal exploitation of minerals.
EU importers must identify and assess, in accordance with Annex II to the OECD Due Diligence Guidance and the specific recommendations set out in that Guidance, the risks in their supply chain based on available third-party audit reports concerning the smelters and refiners in that chain, and, by assessing, as appropriate, the due diligence practices of those in the chain.
Importers of metals must identify, assess, assess and assess risks in the supply chain, based on information available from third parties, the risk in their own supply chain and the risks related to those of the companies in the process of processing the metals, and the risk of the chemicals in that they process.
They must also assess the risks associated with the chemicals they process and the metals they process, and assess the risk associated with those chemicals.
If an EU importer fails to comply with this regulation, it must inform the European Commission of the reasons for its decision and inform the relevant national authorities.
The Commission must adopt a delegated act granting EU countries the power to impose sanctions on importers for infringements of the regulation.
Ex-post checks
EU countries are required to carry out ex-post inspections of importers to check that they are complying with the regulation and that they have carried out the required checks. The checks must be carried out by EU countries’ competent authorities and must be kept for at least 5 years.
National authorities must notify the Commission of any infringements within 3 months of the expiry of the delegated act. The Commission must keep a register of infringements for 5 years of the date of expiry.
In addition, EU countries must establish rules applicable to infringements that are applicable to the import and export of minerals from conflict areas. These rules must be notified to the Commission and the European Parliament and to the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The EU has to incorporate the regulation into national law by 31 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Supply-chain due diligence’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 57:
Customs procedures — placing of goods into free zones
Customs Procedures — putting of goods on the market
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — placing goods into the free zone
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for placing goods in free zones (i.e. the EU’s external borders) in order to avoid customs controls.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all goods entering or leaving the EU.
It does not apply to:
processed products;
re-exports;
exports from the EU to non-EU countries;
transport within the EU;
temporary storage of goods;
goods repaired free of charge.
The rules apply to the following procedures:
Entry summary declaration
The entry summary declaration must be lodged by the carrier of the goods. The goods must be brought into the customs territory of the EU and must be accompanied by an exit summary declaration. The declaration must contain the following information:
the name and address of the carrier;
the date and time of entry into the EU for which the goods are to be brought;
a description of the type of transport and the means of transport;
where the goods have been brought into or are to leave the EU, the date and place of departure for which they will be returned to the country where they were brought.
An export declaration is lodged by a person who has been granted authorisation by the customs authorities for the export of goods from the country of destination.
A customs declaration is issued by the holder of the export notification in the economic operator's computer system. The customs authorities may require that the form of guarantee chosen be maintained for a specific period.
Temporary storage declaration
A temporary storage declaration may be issued by a holder of a customs declaration for goods which have been temporarily stored in the territory of another EU country. The holder of this declaration must keep appropriate records in a form approved by customs authorities.
Implementation of the customs declaration
Implementing Regulation (EU countries must apply the rules laid down in the regulation to implement implementing acts adopted by the EU countries in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 552/2008.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
 * Customs procedure: a procedure whereby goods are placed into a free zone and are subject to customs controls at entry and with regard to records.
* Exit summary declaration: a declaration that allows a person to leave or enter the EU without having to present the goods to customs.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Regulation (Euratom) No 852/2017 of 21 December 2017 on placing goods on a free-zone (recast)

Summary 58:
Customs procedures for the destruction of infringements of intellectual property rights
Customs Procedures for the disposal of infringing intellectual property right
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1215/2012 on the procedure for destruction of goods
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the cooperation between EU countries’ customs authorities in the fight against the sale of counterfeit goods.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the procedures to be followed when goods are suspected of infringing an intellectual property (IP) right in the EU.
It does not apply to goods of a non-commercial nature contained in travellers’ personal luggage.
Implementation of the regulation
Implementing acts
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation by means of implementing acts. These acts must be submitted to the competent customs department of the EU country in which the goods are imported or exported.
The Commission must establish an application form for each EU country’s customs authorities to submit applications to the customs department.
An application must contain all the information necessary to determine whether an IP right has been infringed, including:
the name and address of the declarant or the holder of the decision;
the date and place of sale of the goods;
a description of the customs formalities that have been carried out;
information on the goods and the conditions under which they have been seized;
details of the type and quantity of goods to be destroyed.
A customs official must notify the customs authorities of the information contained in the application within 10 working days of receiving the application.
If an application does not contain the information required, the authorities must inform the applicant of the reason for the decision and the date and time of the declaration of the infringement of the right.
When a customs official receives a request for the declaration or declaration of an infringement of a right, he or she must provide the applicant with all the necessary information, including the name, address and date of sale, the date of the sale or the amount of goods seized and the type of goods destroyed, as well before the declaration is made.
In the event of a failure to provide the information, the customs official may decide to suspend the actions of the authorities until the applicant has provided all the required information.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDING LEGISLATION APPLY?

It has applied since 1 January 2015 and had to become law in the European Union countries by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
EU countries have to ensure that their customs authorities have the necessary resources to deal with counterfeiting and counterfeit goods when they cross borders.
For more information, see:
‘Importers’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 59:
Customs programme — multiannual financial support for customs authorities (2018-2021) (European Commission)
Customs program — multimillion-euro support for Customs authorities (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 883/2013 establishing a new programme, the Customs programme (‘the programme’)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the European Union’s (EU), Euratom, Customs programme to support EU countries’ customs authorities in order to improve cooperation in the field of customs.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the objectives of the programme, which are to:
improve cooperation between the EU and its Member States, to ensure security and safety within the Union and to protect the EU from unfair and illegal trade, while facilitating legitimate business activity;
support the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) in its work to investigate fraud, corruption and any other illegal activity affecting the financial interests of the EU;
provide support for the wider mission of customs authorities, as provided for in Article 3 of Regulation (EU).
The programme is implemented by the European Commission, which is responsible for implementing the programme.
It is funded by the EU budget.
Programme objectives
The specific objectives are to support:
the preparation and coordination of the work of the customs authorities;
the coordination of customs cooperation between EU countries and their Member States;
monitoring, monitoring and control of fraud and corruption;
assistance for the European anti-fraud office;
exchange of information on fraud and smuggling, including on-the-spot checks and inspections;
cooperation in the establishment of a monitoring and evaluation framework.
Funding
The EU budget for the programme is set out in the regulation.
Implementing acts
The European Commission implements the regulation by means of implementing implementing acts. These implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 17(2) of the regulation (see summary).
EU countries must submit their implementing acts to the Commission for approval.
The Commission then adopts implementing acts in line with the implementing acts adopted by the Commission.
Expert groups
The selection of external experts for expert groups is based on Commission Decision of 30 May 2016 establishing horizontal rules on the creation and operation of Commission expert groups. The objective of ensuring a balanced representation of stakeholders and the principle of gender equality should be taken into account when selecting external experts.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
Decision No 884/2013/EU entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Customs programme is part of the European External Action Service (Euratom) programme for the implementation of the common commercial policy and the other common EU policies having a bearing on trade, and to overall supply chain security.
For more information, see:
‘Customs’ on the Euratom website.

Summary 60:
The European Social Fund (ESF+)
The European social fund (ESSF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1060 — rules on the EU’s social fund under shared management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the use of EU countries’ multiannual investment strategies for the implementation of the 20 key principles of the Pillar of Social Rights of the European Union (PSEU).
It aims to improve the functioning of labour markets by supporting the modernisation of labour unemployed people, for disadvantaged groups on the labour market and for inactive people, as well as by means of promoting self-employment and the social economy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Ireland and the United Kingdom (1), which are eligible for the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
EU countries may use the ESF+ strand under their own national investment strategies in support of their reform priorities.
Eligible actions
Actions covered by the regulation are defined in the annexes to the regulation.
The EU countries may support the following actions:
social partners and civil society organisations:
participants in employment, including selfemployment, six months after leaving their job;
people with improved labour market value of goods and services;
persons with material deprivation;
workers’ rights;
social protection and inclusion;
children and adolescents;
young people;
women and men;
the elderly;
inactive people.
Activities covered by Regulation (EU countries must ensure that the actions are carried out in line with the objectives set out in the regulation and that they are coordinated and coordinated with other EU programmes.
Participation of social partners
The social partners must be consulted on the planned use of technical assistance and on other issues having an impact on implementation of strategies at EU level relevant to the programme.
Social partners must also be involved in the development of innovative approaches tested on a small scale and developed under the EaSI strand and other European Union programmes. The social partners may also support the scaling-up of innovative solutions tested at local, regional or national level and transferred to other contexts in different EU countries with financial support from the EU programmes or from other sources.
A working group composed of representatives of the social partners, civil society and the European Commission is responsible for the coordination of the activities of the different strands of the EU social fund.

Summary 61:
EU solidarity programme for young people — 2018-2020 (Solidarity Corps)
EU solidarity program for youth — 2018–2020 (solidarity corps)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/817 — rules for the implementation of the 2018–2022 European Solidarity Corps Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the general and specific objectives of the EU’s solidarity programme ‘2018–2020’, which aims to encourage young people to express their solidarity in practice.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme is open to all young people in the European Union (EU). It aims to:
provide young people with the opportunity to participate in solidarity activities in order to respond to unmet societal needs and contribute to strengthening communities and civic participation;
encourage the participation of individuals and organisations from non-EU countries and territories in solidarity projects;
promote the solidarity of young people across the EU;
support the work of social enterprises;
facilitate the exchange of information and skills between young people and social partners.
Objectives
The objectives are set out in the programme's general objectives and its specific objectives, which are:
to respond to the unmet needs of young Europeans;
to strengthen communities and to strengthen civic participation.
To achieve these objectives, the programme aims to support young people by:
volunteering as part of a host capacity;
performing solidarity activities;
organising solidarity projects and networking activities for individuals, organisations and organisations participating in this strand;
providing support for individuals and groups;
raising awareness of the political priorities of the Union;
improving the quality of life in the EU.
Participating organisations
The participating organisations must meet the following criteria:
participating organisations must:
be a host organisation;
be no-profit in accordance with the Financial Regulation;
have a minimum budget of €500,000 for the duration of the programme;
participate in all the activities covered by the programme.
The national agencies responsible for implementing the programme are responsible for:
coordinating the activities of the participating organisations;
monitoring and evaluating the results of the actions they manage;
developing a consistent strategy with regard to effective outreach, and dissemination and exploitation of results of activities supported under the programme activities.
Financing
The European Union provides the financial support for the actions of participating organisations. The level of support is based on objective criteria, including:
the number of participants;
the quality and support measures taken by participating organisations to achieve the objectives; and
the level of funding provided by the national agencies.
EU countries must ensure that the actions implemented under the EU solidarity programme comply with the rules of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
 
    барскага брака гарка  года  
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
  Барлакка is one of a number of European Union programmes designed to encourage solidarity among young people.

‘EU solidarity’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 62:
Equal opportunities for women and men — European Gender Fund
Equitable opportunities for men and women — European gender fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1371 on the EU’s Equal Opportunities for Women and Men (EGF)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the EU's equal opportunities fund (EGF).
It aims to reduce inequalities and promote equal opportunities between men* and women and to combat discrimination based on sex, racial or ethnic origin, religion or belief, disability, age or sexual orientation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1), which are excluded from the scope of the regulation.
Beneficiaries
EU countries must provide the European Commission with:
a coordinated package of personalised services for women, men and self-employed persons;
information on gender, age group, education level and level of professional experience;
support for self-employment and enterprise creation;
technical assistance at the initiative of the Commission.
The EGF is funded by the European Union budget.
Eligible beneficiaries
The European Commission must identify the beneficiaries of the EGF and provide them with information on:
their employment status;
their level of employment;
the number of hours worked;
number of hours of work;
how they have been employed;
where they have left the labour market;
whether or not they have a job or are unemployed;
they have been unemployed for more than 6 months or have left their job or have been working for less than 12 months.
A beneficiary survey must be open to participation for at least 4 weeks and must be carried out by EU countries’ competent authorities.
National authorities must inform the Commission about the efforts made to contact the beneficiaries.
Each EU country must also provide the Commission with a report on the implementation of EGF. The Commission then forwards the report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee on the Functioning of the Union.
EU governments must ensure that the information they provide to the Commission is accurate and up-to-date and that it is up to date.
In addition, the Commission must provide information on the number of people who have been laid off from their jobs or who have lost their jobs in the context of redundancies.
If the Commission considers that the data provided by an EU country does not meet the criteria for eligibility, it may withdraw the funding.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
    данскабрака барка  гарна года машка has applied since 1 July 2017 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Gender equality: the principle of equal opportunities for all people, irrespective of their sex, race, colour, religion, sexual orientation, disability or age.
* Equal opportunities: the concept of equal opportunity is based on the principle that all people should be treated equally, regardless of their gender, race or sexual identity.
Gender discrimination: the practice of discrimination on the basis of sex or gender based on a person's gender or gender-based characteristics, such as race, sex, colour or religion, age, sex or sex discrimination, age discrimination, disability and age discrimination.

Summary 63:
Interinstitutional Agreement on a mandatory transparency register for the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union (Euratom) and the European Economic and Social Committee of the Regions (EEC) (OJ L 207, 11.6.2021, pp. 1-4)
Interinstitution Agreement on an interinstitutional agreement for the registration of applicants and registrants for the EU’s Transparency Register (EURatom)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/852 concluding the Interinstitutional Agreements on a compulsory transparency register on a voluntary transparency register between the EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies of the EU and the EEC
Agreement between the Euratom, the European Commission and the EU countries on the establishment of the EURatom Transparency Register
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISIONS?
 
The agreement aims to ensure that EU countries’ permanent representatives (applicants) and those who do not represent commercial interests (registrants) have access to the ECR.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ECR is a voluntary register that allows EU countries to register their permanent representatives for the purposes of influencing the formulation or implementation of EU policy or legislation, or the decision-making processes of the signatory institutions or other EU institutions or bodies, bodies or agencies (together referred to as ‘EU institutions’.
It is designed to provide the public and private sectors with information on the activities of EU institutions and bodies.
Eligibility
EU countries must ensure that applicants for registration in the register do not engage in activities with the objective of influencing EU policies or legislation.
Applicants for registration must provide the following information:
their total budget for the most recent financial year closed;
their main sources of funding by category: EU funding, public financing, grants, donations, their members’ contributions etc.;
their email address provided will not be published.
Requests for information must be submitted in writing and must be accompanied by supporting material demonstrating that the information relating to their registration activated, the applicant is eligible to become a registrant.
Registrants that do not have a commercial interest must provide financial information, such as:
the amount of funding they have received;
the number of permanent representations they have made;
a description of the activities they have carried out;
an explanation of the reasons for their non-observance;
details of the types of information they have provided;
information on their contact details and contact details of their permanent representations;
contact details of the persons with authorisation to access the ERC;
any other information that may be relevant to the functioning of the register.
Participation
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the agreement.
FROM WHEN DO THE AGENTS APPLY?
The agreements entered into force on 20 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU is one of the largest trading partners in the world.
For more information, see:
EU transparency register (European Commission).

Summary 66:
Sanctions for euro area countries with excessive government deficits (the Stability and Growth Pact) in the euro area (the Stabilisation and Programme for the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) (SGP) — enhanced surveillance and enforcement
Sanctions on euro-area countries having excessive government deficit
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1466/97 on the enforcement of sanctions for enhancing enforcement of the stabilisation and programme for the functioning of the Stability and growth Pact
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a system of sanctions to enhance the enforcement by EU countries whose currency is the euro to ensure that they comply with the fiscal framework of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to EU countries with a gross domestic product (GDP) of more than 2.5 % of the euro.
It does not apply to countries that do not use the euro or have their national currency in another currency.
EU countries whose GDP is less than 2 % of its GDP must lodge with the European Commission an interest-bearing deposit amounting to 0.2 % of their GDP in the preceding year. The deposit must be lodged with the Commission within 20 days of the Council’s recommendation for the Council to impose sanctions on the country.
The Council may impose fines of up to €500,000 for each country that fails to lodge the deposit.
If the Council adopts a decision establishing that a country has not complied with the Council recommendation, the Commission recommends that the country lodge a deposit of at least €2 million of its own money with the EU country. If the amount of the fine exceeds the amount lodged in accordance with Article 5 of the regulation, or if no deposit has been lodged, the country must make up the shortfall when it pays the fine or abrogates the Council decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION INTO FORCE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 1997 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 1999.
BACKGROUND
The Stability and Stability Pact (SSP) is the cornerstone of the economic and monetary union (e.g. the euro).
It aims to ensure the stability of the monetary union and to ensure its proper functioning.
For more information, see:
‘Stabilisation’ on the European Central Bank's website.

Summary 67:
Population and housing statistics
Population and Housing statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 176/2008 on the production and dissemination of Community statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and transmission of data on the European Union (EU) population, social and economic characteristics of persons, families and households, as well as on housing at a national, regional and local level.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must submit to the European Commission (Eurostat) data on:
the population covering determined demographic, social, economic and gender characteristics;
the housing of persons and families;
relationships between households and households;
social and economic aspects of households and families.
The data must be submitted to Eurostat in electronic form.
Each EU country must submit data on its population and housing data to the Commission on the basis of a national register-based or sample-based population or housing census.
Data collection
The Commission must collect data on all EU countries’ populations and housing at national and regional levels.
Eurostat must transmit the data and metadata to the EU country concerned within 3 months of the date on which the data were collected.
EU country must send the data to Europe’s statistics office, Eurostat, which then forwards them to the national statistical office of the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) in the European Statistical System (ESS).
The ESC is responsible for producing and disseminating the EU statistics.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY?
  ?
The regulation applies from 1 January 2008 to 31 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
The EU countries must send Eurostat data on their populations, households and housing by 1 January 2009.
For more information, see:
‘EU statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Population and Housing Censuses
* EU country:
provides Eurostat with information on the population of EU countries and on the housing of EU citizens;
Housing of persons:
a combination of national and EU-wide surveys;
family units:
social aspects of families
relationship between households
social characteristics of households
relations between households.
MAINTRODUCTIONS
Scope
This regulation applies to the population, housing and social aspects of the EU.
It does not apply to EU countries that do not participate in the ESS.

Summary 69:
European Data Protection Supervisor
European data protection supervisor
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 establishing an independent authority to ensure that the fundamental rights and freedoms of natural persons, and in particular their right to data protection, with respect to the processing of personal data are respected by the European Union institutions, bodies, offices and agencies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European data protection supervisory authority (EDPS) responsible for ensuring that the EU institutions’ data protection policy is respected by natural persons.
It sets out the responsibilities of the EDPS and the European Data Protection Board (EDB).
KEY POINTS
The EDPS is responsible for:
monitoring, investigative and enforcement powers by EU institutions and bodies;
developing and maintaining contact with relevant members and staff of the national supervisory authorities;
exercising and enforcing powers by competent national authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 25 May 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Union has a number of data protection rules in place to ensure the protection of the rights of individuals and their personal data, including:
the protection of their data against fraud and abuse;
protection of their privacy and security; and
protection against abuse of their rights and security.
For more information, see:
European Union’s data protection system (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
 ‘Regulation [EU] 2018/1325 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 May 2018 establishing a European Union data protection authority
Decision (EC) No 132521/2018/EU on the establishment of an independent European data Protection Supervisor (EDP)
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATIONS DO?
Regulations (EU, Euratom) 2019/1721 and (EU 2019/1821 of the EU countries and the EU Agency for Criminal Justice and the Court of Justice of the Eurojust (Euratom) provide for the establishment and operation of the EDPB, the European Directorate-General for the Protection of Personal Data Protection of the Union (EDPB) and the Commission, as well as the European Ombudsman (EU Ombudsman).
The decision establishes the powers of the e-Privacy and Data Protection Bureau (EPDB) to:
ensure that data protection is respected;
monitor, investigate and enforce EU data protection policies;
provide information and advice to the European Commission on data protection issues;
assist the Commission in the implementation of the data protection regulation;
act as a mediator between the Commission and national data protection authorities, in particular in the case of non-compliance by an institution with the regulation, in the event of failure to effectively respond to enforcement action taken by the EPDB;
take decisions on requests for information and other requests for assistance from the Commission;
make recommendations to the Commission on the application of the regulation.
KEY TERMS
* E-Privacy: the term ‘personal data’ is used to refer to personal data.
* Data protection supervisor: the person whose personal data is the subject of a request for information or request for assistance by an EU institution, body, office, office or agency.
ACT
Commission Regulation (EU), Euratom, 2019/2321 of 25 May 2019 establishing a new European data privacy authority (E-Privacy/Data Protection Supervisor) (OJ L 191, 26.5.2019, pp. 1-8)

Summary 70:
The EU’s own resources
The EU's own resources — the EU's share of gross national income
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) No 609/2014 on the system of own resources of the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It sets out the rules for the use of EU countries’ own resources for the financing of the EU.
It aims to ensure that the EU has the financial resources it needs to achieve its objectives.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision sets out rules for:
collecting own resources and making them available to the European Commission;
borrowing funds on capital markets on behalf of the Union for the sole and exclusive purpose of financing measures to address the consequences of the COVID-19 crisis;
receiving own resources from the EU countries.
EU countries must collect own resources by means of the Gross National Income (GNI) keys (i.e. the sum of the gross national incomes (GNIs) of all EU countries, calculated by the Commission, in accordance with Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 549/2013.
The Commission may call on the Member States to provisionally provide the difference between the overall assets and the cash resource requirements, in proportion (‘pro rata’) to the estimated budget revenue of each of them.
A sufficient margin should be preserved under the own resources, which may include a Financial Transaction Tax (FTT) or other taxes, to preserve a sufficient margin to cover the costs of collection.
Borrowed own resources must not exceed 1% of all the Member State's GNI keys.
In addition, the Commission may borrow funds on the capital markets for the purpose of refinancing operations to ensure an efficient debt management strategy.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
 Â  Â
  беъъбебъ  ълъкццешц 
The European Union is the largest economy in the world.
For more information, see:
‘Own resources’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Gross national income: a measure of the value of goods and services produced in each Member State, divided by the total population of the country and divided by its gross domestic product (GDP).
* GNI key: a method of calculating GNI at market prices, based on the respective GNI of the countries and their GNI for each year, without prejudice to other sources of revenue and other revenues.

Summary 71:
Tyres — fuel efficiency and wet grip — information on tyre labels
Tyres— fuel efficiency
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/858 on the labelling of tyres
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out minimum requirements for tyres to be sold in the EU for fuel efficiency, wet grip and external rolling noise.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to tyres sold or offered for sale on the EU’s internal market.
It does not apply to re-treaded tyres or tyres that have been replaced because of tread wear.
EU countries are free to decide on the nature of such incentives.
This regulation is part of a package of measures designed to reduce greenhouse gas emissions (GHG) emissions from road transport and to reduce energy consumption.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
 
It has applied since 26 June 2018 and had to become law in the Member States by 26 June 2022.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Tyre labelling’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Treaded tyre: a tyre that has been replaced or is being replaced because its tread has worn out.
Wet grip: the amount of contact area between the tyre and the road surface.
Rolling resistance: the number of millimetres of the tread of the tyre that can be removed without damaging the tyre.
External rolling noise: the noise emitted by the tyre when the tyre is being driven on the road.
Ice grip: a vehicle is subjected to ice or snow.
MAIN DOCUMENTRYUMENTS
  — Regulation (EU, Euratom):
Directive 2014/40/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 May 2014 on the regulation on the information on the tyre label of tyres sold in EU countries
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATIONS DO?
The legislation sets out the minimum requirements to be met by EU countries for tyres sold on the internal market in the European Union (EU).
It lays down rules on how the information is to be displayed on the labels of tyres and on the pictograms to be included in the product database for each tyre type.
The information on ice grip performance should, once the standard is formally adopted, be based on ISO standard ISO 19447 and the ice grip pictogram should be included on the label of a tyre which satisfies the minimum ice grip index values set out in that ISO standard.
In addition, the regulation sets out how EU countries must ensure that information on wet grip, rolling resistance and the internal rolling noise of tyres is displayed on their websites and on their printed tyre labels.
