Summary 111:
Protocol on the elimination of ozone-depleting substances (Ozone Decontamination Protocol) (OJ C 202, 20.12.2003, pp. 1-8)
Protocol of the European Union on the Elimination of ozone depletion substances (OPT) (OPC) (EUTS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 2023/2003 on the conclusion of the Protocol on the eradication of ozone depleting substances
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE PROTOCOL?
The protocol aims to reduce the risks to human health and the environment caused by the release of harmful substances into the atmosphere from the burning of fossil fuels.
It aims to prevent, reduce and eliminate the production, import, placing on the market, use and export of these substances.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to products and equipment containing or relying on the following substances:
chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs) and hexafluoropropane (OFCs);
chlorinated biphenyls (Dioxins);
nitrous oxide (NOx) and trichloroethylene (TCHs) in construction and demolition waste and in equipment falling within the scope of Directive 2002/96/EC on waste electrical and electronic equipment (WEEE);
bromomethane (Bromohexylpiperazine (BPM) and bisphenol A (BHFCs).
It does not apply to certain substances, such as chlorinated hydrofluoride (HFCPFOs) or hexacrylamide (CHloromethanes, which are banned in the EU.
Products and equipment used for laboratory and analytical purposes must be labelled with the following information:
the name of the manufacturer or importer of the product or equipment;
the quantity of the substance or equipment used in the labelling;
a description of the products or equipment and their intended use.
Labelling
Labels must be clearly visible and clearly visible.
The labelling must include:
a list of the substances covered by the regulation and the names of the importers and importers of those substances;
an indication of the type and quantity of each of the chemicals covered by this regulation;
details of the authorised destruction methods and the conditions for the placing on market of controlled substances for essential laboratory or analytical uses and processes in which controlled substances may be used as process agents.
Manufacturers must ensure that the labels on their products are clearly visible so that end users can identify them.
Importers must also ensure that they have the necessary documentation to prove that they are complying with the regulation.
EU countries may not use substances that are not listed in Annex II of the regulation to manufacture, import or place on the EU market.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
  Закабанска бакка  бъллакова грашка, глагальна   городна,  хаксанна кракланка and года данца власта пробалка have applied since 1 January 2003.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a party to the Montreal Protocol, which aims to eliminate harmful substances from the atmosphere.
For more information, see:
Ozone Degradation Protocol (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Ozone depletion substances: substances that reduce the ozone layer by emitting harmful substances such as carbon dioxide (CO2) and nitrous oxide.
* Chemicals: substances with a potential to emit harmful substances (e.g. HCFCs, HCFC-271, Chlorofluoroethylene, CHloropropanes, BPMs, Bromo-Pyrrolidin, Bisphenols, BCHs, CFCs and CHlorobutyls) that have the potential to release harmful substances in the atmosphere and which have been classified as ‘Ozone Depleting Substances’ in the Protocol.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
 
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 1920/2003 and (EU) No 2101/2003 of the Council and of the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States meeting within the meaning of Article 4(8) of the protocol on the Prohibition of the Emissions of Pollutants from the Extradition of Chemicals from the Production, Importation and Exports of Ozone-Depleting of Substances from the Chemical, Biological, Chemical, Food and Feedstuffs

Summary 113:

European environmental analysis of industry (EAS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2002/EC on the European Environmental Assessment System
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the environmental performance of European Union (EU) companies by improving their environmental performance.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU companies operating in the EU and non-EU countries that have a significant environmental aspect with all applicable legal requirements relating to the environment and have a commitment to continuous improvement of environmental performance, irrespective of the size of their organisation.
It sets out the requirements for organisations to be registered in the European Environment Assessment System (EMASS) and the procedures for their accreditation, licensing and supervision.
EU countries must ensure that organisations are informed about all applicable fees.
Organisations are required to:
provide the European Commission with all relevant environmental information and documentation;
identify their environmental aspects, including their environmental impact and their impact on the environment;
conduct an environmental review of any significant changes to their organisation’s environmental aspects and impacts;
have an internal environmental audit;
ensure that their environmental audit is carried out by a competent body.
The Commission is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of EU countries and the European Accreditation and Licensing Board (EALB).
The European Commission is responsible for administering the EMAS.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO REGULATE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2002.

Summary 115:
Common rules for safeguarding civil aviation security — common basic standards on aviation security (common basic standards for air transport security)
Common rules on safeguarding Civil aviation security
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 — common rules on civil aviation safety
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out common rules for the implementation of the common basic security standards on the security of civil aviation within the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all flights between the EU and non-EU countries.
It does not apply to international flights.
Each EU country must draw up, apply and maintain a national aviation security programme.
Every EU country is responsible for drawing up, applying and maintaining an airport security programme for all flights within its territory.
EU countries may apply more stringent measures than those laid down in the regulation.
Airport operators and other entities are responsible for ensuring that their security measures comply with the regulation’s requirements.
The European Commission is assisted by a committee.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
 
The aim of the regulation is to:
ensure that EU countries’ aviation security measures are effective, proportionate and dissuasive;
protect persons and goods within the EU from unlawful interference with civil aircraft;
improve the safety of passengers and cargo;
provide a high level of security for all passengers and goods and passengers’ personal safety.
For more information, see:
‘Civil aviation security’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Civil aviation:
airport operator: a person, organisation or enterprise or outside airport premises and provide goods and/or services to or through airports referred to in point (a).
* Non-EU country: any EU country that is not a member of the European Economic Area (EEA) or the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development (OECD).

Summary 116:
Frozen funds and economic resources — human rights violations and abuses
Freezing funds and financial resources – human rights abuses
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the freezing of European Union funds
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the measures to be taken by EU countries to prevent, and to punish individuals, entities and bodies responsible for, providing support to or otherwise involved in serious violations or abuses of human rights in the territory of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries and the EU countries’ territories.
It bans the freezing or freezing of funds and the economic resources of, and the prohibition to make funds and resources available to, natural or legal persons, entities or bodies responsible or associated with serious human rights infringements or abuses, as well as those associated with them.
EU countries must inform each other of the measures taken pursuant to this regulation and of other relevant information at their disposal in connection with this regulation.
The EU countries must notify the European Commission of any changes in the addresses of their websites listed in the annexes to the regulation, and of any additional information received directly by the Commission.
National authorities must inform the Commission of their competent authorities, including the contact details of those authorities, without delay after the entry into force of the regulation and must notify it of any subsequent amendment.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMATERIALS APPLY?
Directive (EU countries have to notify the Commission within 2 weeks of the date on which the regulation entered into force.
BACKGROUND
The decision to freeze the funds and to prohibit the release of funds was adopted by the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) on 23 October 2018.
For more information, see:
‘Human Rights and Fundamental Rights’ on the Commission’s website.
KEY TERMS
* Human rights violations: acts that violate the fundamental rights of a person, entity or body, or that are likely to do so.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 117:
EU naval operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAVFORCE IN THE MIDMARY 2020/472 — EU naval operation on the eastern Mediterranean
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020 on a European Union military operation in Libya
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the European Union (EU) to support the EU’s naval mission in Libya with aerial, satellite and maritime assets.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision authorises EU naval operations in Libya to contribute to the implementation of the UN arms embargo on Libya.
The operation is part of the EU military mission in the Eastern Mediterranean, known as EUFOR MEDiterranean (EUFOR MED IRINI).
It is a joint EU-led military operation with the United Nations Support Mission in Libya (UNSMIL), the International Criminal Court (ICC) and the United States of America (US-led International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY).
Objectives
The mission is to:
ensure that the EU is fully involved in enforcing the arms embargo imposed by UNSCR 2146 (2014) on Libya;
support the efforts of the international community to prevent the illicit export from Libya of petroleum, including crude oil and refined petroleum products;
facilitate the training of the Libyan Coast Guard and Navy;
provide technical assistance to the Libyan Armed Forces;
contribute to the monitoring of the situation in Libya and to the stabilisation of the country;
assist in the fight against the proliferation of the armed groups involved in the conflict, including by providing humanitarian assistance;
ensuring that the relevant Libyan authorities are informed of the operation;
carry out inspections of Libyan vessels and vessels;
collect, store and exchange relevant information with the relevant authorities of Member States, competent EU bodies, the UN Support Mission, INTERPOL, the ICC and the US-led Interpol.
EUFORMEDiterranean operations
The EUFORCE
The European Union is responsible for providing the EU naval mission with aerial and satellite surveillance, maritime surveillance and maritime surveillance capabilities, as well as for providing technical assistance.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 March 2020 and had to become law in the EU countries by 31 March 2021.
BACKGROUND
The United Nations has imposed an arms embargo and a travel ban on Libya since 11 February 2014.
For more information, see:
‘Libya’ on the European External Action Service's website.

Council Decision (CFP) 2020/473 of 30 March 2020 on an EU military operation on Libya (EUAFRICOM) (OJ L 101, 31.4.2020, pp. 1-4)

Summary 118:
European Image Archiving System (FADO)
European Image Archive System (EAS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1726 — setting up a European image archiving system for the exchange of information on authentic documents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a system to exchange information on documents and on known methods of falsification between EU countries and the European Union Agency for the Operational Management of Large-Scale IT Systems in the Schengen Area (EU-LISA).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The FADO system was set up to facilitate the exchange and sharing of information between EU Member States and the EU Agency for Law Enforcement Cooperation on authentic and false documents.
It is designed to be a centralised information exchange system for authentic documents and to detect and prevent the falsification of official documents. It should be possible for the system to contain information on other related official documents, in particular those used in support of applications for official documents issued by EU countries, issued by Member States, and on false versions thereof.
EU countries and EU agencies must transmit without delay to the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (the ‘Agency’) the information on the documents referred to in the first subparagraph of the regulation.
The Agency must enter that information in the system and guarantee the uniformity and quality of the information.
Agency responsibilities
The agency is responsible for:
ensuring that the information transmitted to the agency is accurate and up-to-date;
providing the Agency with the information it needs;
assisting the Commission in the implementation of delegated acts;
monitoring the operation of the system;
coordinating the system.
Implementing acts
The Commission adopts the implementing acts on behalf of the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

Summary 121:
Convention on the Law of the Sea — rules for the conservation and management of the living resources of the sea (SPRFMO Convention)
CONVENTION ON THE LAW OF THE SATELLITE OF THE SOUTHEAST MALAYS
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 852/2008 on the conservation of the marine life of the seabed
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the sustainable management of marine life in the South-East Pacific Free Trade Organisation (SPRFMO Convention Area (SPFMO) is done in line with the principles and rules laid down in the United Nations convention on the law of the seas.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
Union fishing vessels operating in the SPRFMO convention area;
third country fishing vessels transhipping fishery products harvested in the convention area.
It does not apply to vessels operating outside the convention agreement.
EU fishing vessels using demersal longlines must use line weights and tori (bird scaring) lines.
Union vessels using bottom fishing footprint longlines are prohibited from discharging offal during shooting and hauling.
All EU fishing vessels must use bottom-finned longlines.
Fishery products must be harvested in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 1005/2008 (see summary).
EU vessels transhipment operations must be accompanied by a flag Contracting Party or CNCP vessel.
If a flag contracting party or a flag of a non-EU country requests access to or being the object of an inspection in an EU port, the vessel must inform the Commission of the vessel’s name, port and port code.
The Commission must forward that information to the Commission, which forwards it to the European Commission Secretariat.
Monitoring of transshipment of jack mackerel and demerals
EU countries must monitor the transhipments of fishery product by vessel and by vessel type by species.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?

Summary 122:
Preventing and deterring the circumvention of the obligation to declare or disclose cash in the EU (Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the control of cash in and out of the EU)
Prevent and determing the circumventing of the obligatory declaration of cash
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 181/2011 — the control and disclosure of cash within the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent and deter criminals and their associates from circumventing the EU’s controls on cash by bringing another type of highly-liquid store of value across its external borders.
KEY POINTS
The regulation requires EU countries to:
require the sender or the recipient of cash to make a disclosure declaration, systematically or on a case-by-case basis, in accordance with national procedures;
make it available to the competent authorities of the country through which they are entering or leaving the EU and make it available for control.
The declaration must contain the following elements:
the origin, destination, economic provenance and intended use of the cash;
the name and address of the person or body responsible for the declaration;
a description of the type of cash and the name of the persons or bodies responsible for it.
A declaration must be provided in writing or electronically, using the disclosure form referred to in Article 16(1) of the regulation.
An endorsed copy of the declaration is delivered to the declarant upon request.
EU countries’ competent authorities must inform the European Financial Action Task Force Units (FATFUses) and the European Anti-Fraud Office (EFUsas) of any information they receive from the EU countries.
If the authorities of an EU country suspect that cash is being used for criminal activities that adversely affect the financial interests of the European Union (EU), they must:
take measures to prevent or disclose it;
ensure that it is returned to the country where it was received;
impose a ban on the movement of cash.
In addition, EU countries must also provide the European Commission with:
anonymised risk information and risk analysis results on cash movements;
an analysis of the risks associated with the movements of cash across EU borders;
information on exchange of information with non-EU countries;
reports on the information received from EU countries regarding cash related to criminal activities which adversely affect their financial interests.
Exchange of information between EU countries and with the Commission
The Commission must publish statistical information on its website.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATED APPLY?
 
It has applied since 2 December 2011 and had to become law in EU countries by 2 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
   
  * KEY TERMS
 * Cash: a highly liquid, highly-quantitative form of cash, such as coins or banknotes, which can be used for a wide range of illicit activities, including money laundering, drug trafficking, human trafficking and money laundering for terrorist financing.
* Currency: a commodity that can be converted into cash by means of a standardised way that allows for quick verification of their value.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission delegated Regulation (Euratom), Euratom of 2 November 2011 laying down the essential components of the definition of ‘cash’ and amending Regulation (EC) No 1889/2005 on the controls of cash (OJ L 309, 25.11.2011, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euroatom) have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 123:
EU energy and climate progress report
EU energy & climate report
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 517/2014 on the EU’s integrated national energy report
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for EU countries’ reporting obligations under the EU Energy Union.
It aims to ensure that EU countries report on the progress they have made in achieving their national objectives, targets and commitments in the field of energy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
EU countries must submit their national progress reports to the European Commission by 30 September every year.
The report must include information on:
energy security; the internal energy market; energy efficiency; decarbonisation; and research, innovation and competitiveness.
Each EU country must also provide the Commission with:
the national objective, target or contribution for each of the five dimensions of the Energy Union;
the energy savings requirement for the whole period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2030;
measures to achieve other national targets, where applicable;
information on the policies and measures adopted, or intended to be adopted and implemented, in order to achieve the objectives;
a description of how they will cover the gap between their own annual savings rate and their national targets;
an assessment of how the national targets will be met.
Annexes to the regulation provide information on the national progress report, including:
how they will meet the energy savings requirements;
energy efficiency;
their national targets for the year before the deadline for submitting their progress reports;
details of the measures they have adopted or intend to adopt to achieve their objectives and targets.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The Energy Union’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Energy Union: the EU's energy policy is based on a set of objectives, policies and actions to reduce greenhouse gas emissions (GHGs) and to reduce the total concentration of GHGs in the atmosphere.

Summary 124:
Contaminants listed in Annex III of the Convention on Pollution from Chemicals
Contaminant lists in Annex 3 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — Listing of substances listed in the Annex III to the Convention of the International Atomic Energy Community
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the list of substances covered by the convention on pollution from chemicals (Annex III) which are banned in the EU.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 850/2004 on persistent organic pollutants.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all substances covered in the annexes to the convention. It does not apply to substances that are not listed in its annexes.
The annexes are:
Annex I: Listing the substances listed by the EU countries
Listing the chemicals covered by Annex II
Listings of the chemicals listed by EU countries in Annex IV
Listening to the public
EU countries must:
identify, characterise and minimise, with a view to eliminating where feasible as soon as possible, the total releases of substances from their inventories as recorded in their national implementation plans;
report to the European Commission, the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work (EASW) and the European Environment Agency (EEWA) on their measures to reduce the total release of chemicals from their stockpiles;
ensure that their national inventories are up-to-date;
inform the EASWaste and lead-containing wastes and lead residues of the non-ferrous metals used in metal production;
provide the EAWA with information on their national action plans.
Each EU country must submit a national implementation plan to the Commission and the EEWA by 16 July 2021.
National implementation plans must include:
a list of the substances to be eliminated or reduced from the EU’s inventories;
a description of the measures to be taken to reduce their total releases;
measures to reduce or eliminate total releases from the inventories of chemicals listed in annexes I and II;
annexes III and IV of the convention;
the list of chemicals to be reduced or eliminated from EU countries’ inventories.
Implementation plans must be updated every 3 years and must include information on:
the substances listed and the measures they have taken to eliminate or reduce them;
their impact on the environment and human health;
information on the substances they have removed or reduced;
how they have reduced or reduced their impact on human health and the environment;
details of the types of substances that have been removed from EU inventories and measures taken to minimise their impact;
methodologies for identifying, characterising and minimising the releases of chemicals and wastes;
procedures for identifying and characterising the substances in Annex I and annexes III;
implementation plans for identifying substances in annex IV;
national implementation plans for each of the four annexes;
monitoring of chemicals in Annexes III, IV, V and VI;
control of manufacturing, placing on the market and use, and the listing of substances;
reporting to the EU and EAWW on the quantities and quantities of substances released;
disputes between EU countries and the Commission on the number of substances and the quantities of wastes that are being released.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECAST APPLY?
Directive 2017/884/EU has applied since 16 July 2017 and had to become law in the Member States by 18 July 2018.
Regulations (EU, Euratom) No 552/2014 and (EU), Euratom No 553/2014 have applied since 18 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
The convention on Pollutants is one of the most important environmental instruments in the world.
For more information, see:
Chemical substances (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Chemicals: substances which are present in mixtures or articles where they have a concentration of more than 500 mg/kg where they are present as a whole or in a mixture of mixtures and articles, subject to review and assessment by the Commission.
* Pollutant: any substance that is present in a mixture or article where it has a concentration greater than 500mg/kg or where it is present as part of a mixture or article, where the concentration of those substances is greater than 0.5 µg/kg.
ACT
Commission Implementing Decision (EU/C 12/06 of 16 July 2016 on the Listing and Monitoring of Chemicals in Annex II of the Protocol on Polluter-Related Substances to the Agreement on the Combating of Pollution by Chemicals (OJ L 55, 2.7.2016, pp. 1-2)

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic products — reducing plastic waste
Single-used plastic products— reducing plastic wastes
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2011/83/EU on reducing plastic pollution
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce plastic pollution in the European Union (EU) by reducing the amount of plastic waste from single-use products.
It lays down rules on how EU countries should tackle the problem of plastic pollution.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all plastic products that are used in short-lived applications, which are not designed for re-use or cost-effective recycling. It does not apply to products intended to be used more than once or for a short period of time before being disposed of.
EU countries are required to set up separate collection systems for these products and to ensure that they are collected and disposed of in a way that minimises the impact of the plastic on the environment.
The legislation applies to the following products:
beverages, such as beverage containers, that are made of plastic;
tampons for personal care and domestic use;
wet wipes;
personal care and hygiene products;
bags for professional uses and personal care;
cigarette filters with filters containing plastic.
Products may only be placed on the market if they fulfil specific product design requirements that significantly reduce the dispersal into the environment of beverage container caps and lids.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Decision (EU countries had to incorporate the directive into national law by 31 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
.
   
It has applied since 1 December 2011 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 December 2012.
For more information, see:
‘single-use plastics’ on the European Commission’s website.

EU trade marking registration and enforcement — codifying Regulation (EU) No552/06
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation on the registration of the EU trade mark
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the application for EU trade marks and the enforcement of those marks in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to applications for trade marks, certification marks or guarantee marks.
It does not apply to:
designation of goods or services;
protection of intellectual property rights (IP rights) in the EU;
recognition of trade marks in non-EU countries;
certification of trade mark systems in EU countries.
Decision-making procedure
The European Union Trade Marks Office (EUTMO) is the EU’s trade mark office.
The EUTMO is responsible for the registration, enforcement and enforcement of the trade marks of goods and services and for the protection of intellectual properties rights in the EEC. It is assisted by the European Patent Office (EPO) and the Office for the Protection of Intellectual Property Rights (OPR) of the European Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION ENTER INTO FORCE?
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2006.
BACKGROUND

For more information, see:

KEY TERMS
* Trade mark: a trade mark, certification mark or guarantee mark.

Summary 127:
Accessible formats for cultural materials for persons with disabilities
Accessible format for cultural material for persons who are blind, visually impaired or otherwise print-disabled
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — making and distributing accessible format copies of certain works and other protected subject matter
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the availability of accessible formats for certain works in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats, of certain copyright and related rights for the benefit of beneficiaries of the Marrakesh Treaty.
It requires contracting parties to provide for exceptions or limitations to copyright or related rights in order to make accessible formats available to beneficiaries.
Accessibility formats include, for example, Braille, large print, adapted e-books, audio books and radio broadcasts.
Beneficiary persons may import or otherwise obtain or access and thereafter use an accessible format copy of a work or other subject matter that has been distributed, communicated or made available to beneficiary persons or to authorised entities, by an authorised entity in a non-EU country that is a party to the treaty.
Authorised entities may also import or use, in accordance with the national legislation adopted pursuant to this regulation, an accessible version of the work or subject matter for non-commercial purposes.
An authorised entity may import, communicate or make available to a beneficiary person or an authorised person in an accessible formats made in the EU country for noncommercial purposes for the purpose of distributing, communicating, communicating or making available to the beneficiary person in the same subject matter.
The EU country may also allow for the importation of, and access to, accessible formats from a third country, by beneficiary persons in an EU country and authorised entities established in a EU country.
This regulation does not apply to:
accessible format copies made in a third EU country that are not part of the EU;
accessible format copies that are imported or otherwise obtained or accessed in an authorised EU country by a beneficiary or authorised entity that is not a member of the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) or the Committee for the Protection of the Rights of Persons with Disabilities (CPSD) in the context of the implementation of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU) and the EU’s common foreign and security policy (CFSPD).
Authorisation of authorised entities
Authorising entities must ensure that:
authorised entities do not use the personal data of beneficiary persons referred to in Article 6.3 of the regulation;
the processing of personal data carried out within the framework of this regulation shall be carried out in compliance with Directives 95/46/EC and 2002/58/EC.
EU countries must introduce a mandatory exception to certain rights that are harmonised by EU law.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
    данска барка  граксанна глабакка, гълак года варанка and гганца канта доба марцка have applied since 11 October 2019 and had to become law in the Member States by 11 October 2023.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve access to cultural materials, including:
Directive 2017/1564 on the protection of intellectual property rights (IPR) for the dissemination of works and related subject matter (see summary);
Regulations (EU, Euratom) 2018/856 and (EU), 2018/906 on the access to information and education of persons who have disabilities;
Commission Regulation (EU/2018/907 on the distribution of cultural materials to persons who do not have disabilities (See summary).
For more information, see:
‘Access to cultural material’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 128:
Coordinated enforcement of cross-border infringements of consumer products — EU rules (Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on coordinated enforcement of infringements
Cooperative enforcement of inter-EU infringements on consumer products
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 — coordinated enforcement measures for crossborder crossborder infringement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the enforcement of widespread infringements* of consumers’ goods and services in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
It does not apply to the EU’s single market for consumer products (SMEs).
Cooperation between EU countries
EU countries must ensure that coordinated enforcement actions are carried out by EU countries’ competent public enforcement authorities (known as ‘coordinated actions’).
The coordinated actions are designed to:
ensure that infringements are detected and brought to an end as quickly as possible;
provide information on suspected infringements;
promote cooperation between the EU countries and the European Commission;
reduce administrative burdens for EU countries;
increase the effectiveness of the coordinated actions;
improve the efficiency of the enforcement procedures;
facilitate the exchange of information between authorities.
Coordination of coordinated actions
The European Commission is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY?
Decision (EU countries have to incorporate the regulation into national law by 1 January 2020.
Directive 2013/2014/13/EU introduces a new penalty regime for infringements covered by this regulation.
Regulation 2013/14/EU amends Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004 to set up a new EU-wide penalty regime.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a network of national enforcement authorities, known as the ‘cross-border enforcement network’, which is responsible for coordinating enforcement actions across the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Cross-border infringement’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Crossborder infringement: an intra-EU infringement occurs when a trader sells a product or service to a consumer in another EU country and then resells it to another consumer in the same EU country.

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices — safety and health — in vitro diagnostics
In vitro diagnostic medical devices
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/745 on the European Union’s framework for in vitro diagnostic devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that in vitro medical devices (i.e. medical devices that can be used to test for genetic diseases) are safe and safe for human use.
It sets out minimum requirements for manufacturers, distributors, importers and distributors of these devices.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all devices that are used for diagnostics, improvement of treatment, predictive or prenatal testing. It does not apply to devices for self-testing.
Manufacturers must draw up and keep up to date the technical documentation for those devices. The technical documentation must include:
the type of specimen(s) required to perform the test (e.g. blood, urine or saliva);
the labelling of the device;
the name of the labelling carrier;
contact details for further advice and assistance.
The labelling must bear the following information on the device:
type of device (class A and class B devices packaged and labelled individually, the UDI carrier must not be required to appear on the packaging but it must appear on a higher level of packaging;
class C and class D devices must be labelled individually and bear their own UDI.
Labels for devices for non-self-testing kits must bear:
name of the laboratory;
labelling of kits;
description of the test(s);
name and contact details;
number of samples required for the test;
label for devices that self-test;
name, address and telephone number of laboratories;
date of entry into force for the device in the EU country in which it is to be tested;
authorisation to use the device.
EU countries must ensure that:
manufacturers draw up a summary of safety and performance of their devices;
they notify the European Medicines Agency (EMA), the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and the European Commission of any modifications to their devices that may affect the safety or health of human beings.
They must also notify the competent national authorities of the modifications they have made to the devices and notify them of the reasons for and the nature of those modifications.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?

Summary 130:
Electrical appliances and fittings — EU conformity assessment — rules
Electric appliances and fitting — European conformity assessment rules
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the European Union’s conformity assessment of electrical appliances
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the EU’S conformity assessment system for electrical appliances* and their fittings.
It aims to ensure that the safety of consumers and other end-users in the EU is ensured by harmonised rules on the sale and putting into service of these products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all electrical appliances, including refrigerators, air conditioners, washing machines, dishwashers and washing machines.
Importers must ensure that:
the appliance is accompanied by instructions and safety information in a language easily understood by consumers, as determined by the EU country in which it is sold;
the packaging appliance contains all necessary information for adjustment, operation and maintenance of the fittings as part of the finished appliance, as appropriate.
Manufacturers must make available on the market all the necessary information on the appliance and its fittings, including the name of the manufacturer and the contact details.
Distributors must notify the competent national authorities when they place an appliance or fitting on the EU market.
The European Commission is assisted by the Committee on appliances.
Directive 2009/142/EC is based on the ‘new approach’ principles, as set out in the Council Resolution of 7 May 1985 on a new approach to technical harmonisation and standards.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDING INTO FORCE
   
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2013.
BACKGROUND
The directive is part of a package of measures designed to modernise and harmonise EU rules on technical standards for the sale, putting in place a system for the harmonisation of technical standards and harmonising the sale of products. It aims to improve the quality of products and reduce administrative burdens for consumers.
For more information, see:
‘Electrical and electronic appliances’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 131:
Cash-in-transit (CIT) — cross-border transport of euro cash by road between participating EU countries (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Cash cash-in–transit — cross–EU transport
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EEC) No 552/2011 on the cross–border carriage of euro banknotes and euro coins by road in the euro area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the cross‑border carriage by road of cash in–cash transport of euros by road across the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the cash in‑transit service provided by a cash in‐transit company.
It does not apply to banks, the large retail sector and other professional cash handlers that offer the best price and/or service and to take advantage of the cash services of the nearest national central bank (NCB) branch or CIT cash centre, even if it is located in another EU country.
EU countries must ensure that:
CIT vehicles are equipped with a global navigation system (GNSS) to ensure the security of their vehicles;
the CIT security staff wear bulletproof vests during the transport and must wear them where required by the law of the EU country where they are situated.
The CIT vehicle must be equipped with an end-to-end IBNS to ensure that it can be programmed in a secure location.
A CIT company must apply for a CIT licence from the country in which it is established.
An EU country’s national inspection authority issues the licence and checks the CIT companies’ compliance with the regulation.
Each EU country must designate one or more inspectors to carry out inspections of CIT vehicles to check that they comply with the rules of the regulation and that they carry out the checks correctly.
If an EU country considers that an inspection has been carried out incorrectly, it must inform the other EU countries and the European Commission of its decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The delegated regulation has applied since 27 October 2011 and had to become law in the EU countries by 27 October 2012.
BACKGROUND
The euro area is the largest single currency area in the world.
For more information, see:
Cross-border cash transport (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* CIT: a cash transport company that carries out cross–currency transport by road.
* Cash in‐cash transport: the transport of cash by cash in a vehicle to and from a cash centre.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 132:
Safe and wholesome foods — labelling
Safe and healthy foods — nutrition labelling			
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2011 on the labelling of foods
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the presentation of food information on the EU’s internal market.
It aims to ensure that food information is accurate, clear and easy to understand for consumers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all foods sold in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to:
processed foods;
handcrafted foods, including handcrafted food, directly supplied by the manufacturer of small quantities of products to the final consumer or to local retail establishments directly supplying the final consumers;
pre-packaged foods, such as frozen meat, frozen meat preparations and frozen unprocessed fishery products;
frozen fish and shellfish, including frozen fish preparations, frozen fish products and frozen fish stocks;
seaweed, seaweed and seaweed products, including seaweed, shellfish and mussels;
meat, poultry and fish products.
Labelling
Foods must be labelled with:
the energy value and the amount of nutrients and/or the percentage of the reference intakes set out in Part B of Annex XIII of the regulation;
the amount of fat, saturates, sugars, sugars and salt in the form of an indication;
a description of the ingredients;
an indication of the composition of the foodstuffs used;
foods or products intended for human consumption.
The labelling must be accurate and clear for the consumer;
information on potential allergens or products causing or likely to cause allergies or other adverse reactions must be provided to the consumer.
Food information must be accompanied by:
a list of ingredients, including the name or address of the manufacturer or the name of a food business operator;
instructions for the preparation, storage, transport and handling of the product, including a description of how the product is prepared;
description of the products used for preparation, transport, storage and handling;
details of the substances used for processing, storage or handling the product;
disclosure of the names or addresses of the persons responsible for preparing, transporting and handling the food;
descriptions of ingredients used in preparation, packaging and handling, including an indication of their composition and the quantities of those substances used in the preparation and packaging;
indications of the presence or absence of substances or substances intended to cause allergy or adverse reactions;
allergen indications, including indications related to allergens and products causing allergies and products likely to present adverse reactions to human consumption;
advertisements for products intended to be prepared, transported or handled by a person who is allergic or has been exposed to an allergy or allergy-causing substance;
advertising for foods intended for consumption by persons who are not allergic or who have not been exposed or have not had exposure to an adverse reaction to a substance or substance.
EU countries may adopt national measures concerning matters not specifically harmonised by this regulation. They must communicate to the European Commission the text of those measures without delay.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 12 December 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 12 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission publishes a list of foods covered by the regulation on its website.
For more information, see:
Nutrition labelling (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Allergen indication: an indication indicating that a food may contain a substance that may cause an allergy, a substance causing a reaction or a reaction to human or animal consumption, or a substance likely to produce adverse reactions.
* Nutritional indication: a label that indicates that a product may have a beneficial physiological effect, a harmful physiological effect or a potential adverse reaction.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 		````
 
  ‘Regulation 2011/552/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 November 2011 on food information’ (OJ L 183, 12.12.2011, pp. 1-8)

Summary 133:
Issuing of euro coins and 2-euro commemorative coins (circulation and collector coins)
Issuance of euro notes and 2 euro commemoratives
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1210/2010 on the issuance of euro coin and 2/euro commemororative coins
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the issuing of 1-euro coins and euro collector coins.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries whose currency is the euro.
It does not apply to non-euro countries.
EU countries may issue one or more types of coin:
circulation coins* intended for circulation, the denominations and technical specifications of which are laid down in Regulation (EC) No 975/98;
collector coins* that are intended for collection that are not issued with a view to their entry into circulation.
Issues of circulation coins
Each issuing EU country may issue two types of coins:
1-euro circulation coins, which are intended to commemorate a specific subject, subject to limits set per year and per issuing country for the number of issues of such coins;
2-euro collector coins* which are issued to commemorate specific subjects, subject on the basis of a ceiling set by the issuing country.
The issuing country may not exceed the higher of the following two ceilings:
0,1 % of the cumulated total net number of 2-Euro coins put into circulation by EU countries up to the beginning of the year preceding the end of the calendar year for each individual issue;
0.5 % of cumulated net numbers of 2 euro coins issued by each issuing country up to end of calendar year.
Collector coins may be put on the market at or above face value. They must not have a shaped edge with fine scallops or a ‘Spanish flower’ shape.
Consultation prior to the destruction of circulating coins
Before the euro coins which are not euro coins unfit for circulation are destroyed, EU countries must consult each other via the relevant subcommittee of the Economic and Financial Committee and inform the mint directors of the Member States whose currency has the same denomination and specifications.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDITIONS APPLY?

Summary 134:
The European Social Fund (ESF) and the Youth Employment and Employment Instrument (YEI)
The European social fund (E&EI)*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 on the European Social Facility
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s role and scope of the European social funds (ESFs).
It lays down specific provisions concerning the type of activities that may be financed by the ESF.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ESF supports projects that aim to:
promote social inclusion and combating poverty;
combat poverty and social exclusion;
improve the quality of life of disadvantaged people;
fight social exclusion and promote equal opportunities.
It is funded by the European Union (EU).
Objectives
The objectives of the E&EIs are to promote social inclusion, combating poverty and combating social exclusion by:
encouraging young people to participate in the labour market;
promoting health and safety at work and increasing employability;
improving education and training for young people, including in the field of vocational training;
supporting training and social inclusion;
developing the skills of young people in order to improve their employability and employability.
Eligibility for funding
The EU countries must designate a priority axis for each of the thematic objectives.
The priority axes for each thematic objective is set out in the regulation.
EU countries must provide the European Commission with the information on the priority axis and the EU countries’ investment priorities for each priority axis by 31 December 2015.
Each priority axis must have a budget of at least €500 million for the year ending 31 December 2016.
In order to achieve the objectives, EU countries have to provide the Commission with all the information it needs to determine how to finance the priority axes.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 31 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
The E&Es are part of a package of measures designed to help EU countries tackle poverty, social exclusion, social inequality and poverty alleviation.
For more information, see:
European Social Funds (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS
 
   
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1301/2013 of 30 November 2013 on the EU Social Fund and Regulation (EC) No 1081/2006 of 31 October 2006 laying down the rules for the European Parliament and of the Council on the establishment, operation, management and control of European Social Funds

Summary 135:
Statistics on population and on vital events — EU countries’ data on the European Union (EU) — European statistical system (NUTS)
Statistical data on population
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1059/2003 of the European Parliament and of the Council on the EU’s annual demographic statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out how EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with statistics on the total population of the EU and on the number of deaths and births in the EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries which are broken down by territorial units (border regions, metropolitan regions, rural regions and mountain and island regions.
It applies to the data transmitted by EU countries to Eurostat.
EU countries are required to provide the Commission with data on:
the total population at national level at the reference time, in accordance with Article 2(c), within eight months of the end of the reference year;
the number of births and deaths by age, sex, year of birth, region of residence, country of birth and country of citizenship and month of occurrence.
The data must be transmitted to the Commission using the NUTS classification, which is based on the data sources, definitions and estimation methods used for the first reference year and the second reference year, and the third reference year.
Eurostat must send the data to the European Statistical System (ESS) for each year within 8 months of its end, as appropriate.
the data must include:
live births by sex, month of birth or death, mother's age, father's age or country of residence and mother's country of nationality;
number of deaths by death, month or year of death, death or death;
deaths by age or death by region or region;
dates of births or deaths by sex or region of birth;
date of death by death by country or region or death.
Data on population, including vital events, must be sent to the ESS within 8 weeks of their end. The reference period for vital events data is the calendar year in which the events occurred.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013 and had to become law in EU countries by 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Annual demographic statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Total population: the total number of people living in an EU country, divided by its population at the time of its entry into force.

Summary 137:
Conservation of marine biological resources — Common Fisheries Fund (CFP) (European Maritime and Fisheries Fund) (EMFF)
Convention of the European Parliament and of the Council on the Conservation of Marine Biological Resources (CFCR) (Euratom) (EC) No 1380/2013)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 on the common fisheries fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s common fisheries finance (CFF) policy, which aims to support EU countries’ efforts to protect the marine ecosystems of the Atlantic Ocean and the Pacific Ocean.
It aims to help EU countries achieve the objectives of the Convention on Biological Diversity (CBD) by providing financial support for the design and implementation of conservation measures and regional cooperation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1).
It does not apply to non-EU countries.
EU countries may apply for financial support under the CFP if they:
have not complied with their obligations under the regulation;
have failed to fulfil their obligations;
are in breach of their CFP obligations; or
have committed a serious non-compliance with the regulation.
Funding
The CFP is funded by the European Union (EU), the European Commission, the European Economic and Social Fund (ESF and the European Fisheries Fund.
The EMFF is the main instrument of the EU provides financial support to EU countries in the field of marine conservation of biological resources (MBSR).
The EU countries are required to:
ensure that their fisheries are managed in a way that minimises the impact of their activities on the marine environment;
promote the sustainable use of their marine resources;
encourage the sustainable management of their fisheries;
support the development of innovative approaches and practices that are capable of simple and transparent implementation;
provide technical assistance;
facilitate the implementation of operational programmes;
foster the transfer of knowledge between scientists and fishermen;
contribute to sustainable growth.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY PROCEDURE APPLY?
Decision 2014/852/EU has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The Common Fisheries Funds are the cornerstone of EU fisheries policy.

Summary 138:
Pension products — key information document
Pensions — key Information document
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 on the disclosure of product information requirements for personal pension products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down minimum requirements for retail investors to be provided with information on the sale of personal pension investment products (PRIIPs) in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all retail investment products that are marketed or sold to investors in the EU.
It does not apply to insurance-based investment products, such as life insurance, which are not covered by this regulation.
EU countries must ensure that retail investors have access to the information they need to make a decision on a PRIIP before they buy it.
PRIIP manufacturers must provide the retail investor with:
a summary of the product’s main features;
a description of the risks associated with the product;
an explanation of how the product works;
information on the risks related to the product.
The information must be provided in a language that is understandable and understandable to the consumer.
This information must include:
the name of the manufacturer or the person advising on selling the product and the contact details of the person selling the PRIIPs;
the contact details for the person who is responsible for marketing the product to the investor;
procedures for obtaining the information;
details of the price of the products;
how the product is traded on the secondary markets.
the retail investor must be informed of any additional information documents that may be provided by the manufacturer and/person selling the products, including the price, the price and the number of shares traded, the product has in the secondary market and the amount of money invested.
Provision of the key information documents
The EU countries must publish the information in their annual reports. They must also ensure that any publication, in accordance with this regulation, on their official website, remains on the official website for at least 5 years after the date of entry into force of the regulation and for as long as the products are traded on secondary markets
The European Securities and Markets Authority (ESAs) must publish a summary of all the information that they provide to retail investors on their website.
They must also publish a copy of each document on the ESAs’ website. The ESAs must publish this summary on the website of the national authorities responsible for the supervision of the marketing of the retail investment product in their EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
    б  
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
  —

Summary 139:
Bail-in and resolution of failing banks — resolution of insolvent entities
Bail - in and out of insolvency
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 567/2014 on the establishment of the European System of Resolution Mechanism (SRM)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European system of resolution for failing banks in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that the EU’s financial sector is able to cope with the impact of the 2008 financial crisis and its aftermath.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU banks that have been declared insolvent by national authorities.
It does not apply to banks that are part of a group of banks or that are not part of the EU.
Resolution
The resolution of a failing bank must be carried out by the national authorities responsible for the resolution of the failing bank.
A resolution plan must be submitted to the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and the European Bank Resolution Board (EBA).
The EBA is responsible for assessing whether the resolution plan meets the objectives of the resolution procedure.
National authorities must submit to the EBA all information necessary to draw up and implement the resolution plans, as obtained by them in accordance with Article 11 and Article 13(1) of Directive 2014/59/EU, without prejudice to this regulation.
The European Commission must inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the results of the national resolution authorities’ investigations into the resolution procedures.
EU countries must notify the European Central Bank of any cooperation arrangements they have concluded with the European Investment Bank (EIBB).
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
  
It has applied since 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
 			
For more information, see:
‘Resolution of banks’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 140:
Noise-related operating restrictions at European Union (EU) airports — Regulation (EC) No 852/2004
Noise - related operating restrictions in the European Union
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 802/2004 on the introduction of noise-related operational restrictions at EU airports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on how EU countries can introduce operating restrictions to reduce the noise of aircraft flying over EU airports.
It aims to improve the noise environment around EU airports by removing the noisiest aircraft from the EU’s airspace.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to aircraft engaged in civil aviation. It does not apply to aircraft operating in non-EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that:
airport authorities are independent of any organisation which could be affected by the action;
noise performance information is provided for noise modelling purposes.
Airport authorities must put in place the necessary monitoring tools.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
When an EU country introduces an operating restriction, it must notify the EU countries, the Commission and the relevant interested parties within 6 months, ending at least 2 months prior to the determination of the slot coordination parameters for the airport concerned for the relevant scheduling period.
An EU country must inform the European Commission of the measures it intends to take.
Aircraft noise and performance information must be provided to the competent authorities for the purpose of assessing the impact of the action.
If an airport is subject to a noise restriction, EU countries must provide the Commission with:
a list of the airports in which are subject to the operating restrictions
the number of aircraft that have been issued a type certificate
the type certificate for which has been issued
the location of the airport
the duration of the operating restriction
a description of the impact on the health of people and the environment
measures to be taken by the EU country
the cost of the measure
the amount of the economic incentives to be paid by the affected country
measures that may be taken to reduce noise at source
measures which may be used to abate the effects of the noise
The Commission must publish that information.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 July 2004.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Airport noise’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Operating restrictions: measures that restrict the movement of aircraft at an airport to reduce their noise.

Summary 141:
Cross-border insolvency proceedings — common rules for creditor information and communication in civil and commercial insolvencies (Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
Cross-Border insolvence proceedings — Common rules for creditors information and communications in civil & commercial insolventcies (Commission Regulation (EU))
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) No 852/2018 on the application of certain provisions of the European Union’s common rules on insolventcy proceedings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out common rules to ensure that creditors who have their habitual residence, domicile or registered office in the EU are informed about the opening of insolvences relating to their assets.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies only to proceedings in respect of a debtor whose centre of main interests is located in the Union. It does not apply to proceedings which involve only the financial creditors of the debtor.
It does not affect national rules on recognition and enforcement of arbitral awards.
Creditors have the right to lodge claims for their assets and to withdraw their claims where the law of the EU country in which the debtor has an establishment does not allow them to do so.
The procedure for lodging claims must be established in accordance with the standard claims form established in Article 88 of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU).
The creditor may lodge a claim in a national court in another EU country if:
the creditor is not located in that country;
the debtor has not moved to that country by the time the creditor lodges its claim;
claims are lodged in the claim in the country where the creditor is located;
there is a dispute between the creditor and the debtor over the lodgement of the claim.
Procedure for lodging the claim
The debtor must lodge its claim using the standard claim form established by Article 88.
If the creditor has moved to a country other than that of the one in which he or she is located, the creditor must lodge his or her claims in the jurisdiction of the court which delivered that decision. The creditor must be able to participate in the vote by distance means of communication, where national law so permits.
Where the creditor moves to a new country, the debtor may lodge his/her claims in that new country. If the creditor does not move, he/she must lodge them in the same country as the one where the debtor is located.
When a debtor moves to another country and the centre of its main interests are not located on its territory, it must not lodge its claims in its country of residence.
However, if the creditor decides to move to a non-EU country, it may lodge claims in a country where its centre of interests are located, provided that:
it has moved within the EU;
it is not subject to the laws of the country of origin of the creditor; and
the law of that country does not permit any means of challenging that act in the relevant case.
In addition, if a creditor moves from one EU country to another, they may lodge their claims in their home country, provided they do not have the necessary authorisation from the court in which they are lodged.
EU countries must ensure that the court with which a request for group coordination proceedings has been filed makes an assessment of those criteria prior to opening group coordination procedures.
Cooperation and communication
The European Commission has set up a European Network for the Coordination of Civil and Commercial Insolvency Arrangements (ENCCA) to facilitate the exchange of information on cross-border proceedings.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2014/13/EU has applied since 1 July 2014 and had to become law in all EU countries by 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to modernise its legal framework for the application and management of insolvent cases.
For more information, see:
‘Interconnecting the EU’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
    ‘Regulation of the Council and of the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States, meeting within the Council, of 1 July 2018 on the implementation of the Common Rules on the Application and Management of Insolvent Proceedings (COM(2014) 852 final - not published in the Official Journal)’
Commission Decision 2014/14/EU of 12 July 2014 on the harmonisation of the rules on the operation of the common framework on the coordination of civil insolvent proceedings (OJ L 347, 14.7.2014, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) No 454/2014 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

  “Regulation 2014/12/EU on the interconnection of the national insolvent registers of the Union



Summary 142:
Athens Convention for the protection of passengers in the event of maritime accidents (OJ L 183, 29.12.2004, pp. 1-8)
Athens convention for the provision of protection of persons in the case of maritime accident
Athena Convention
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 1999/468/EC on the conclusion, on behalf of the European Community, of the Athens Convention on the Protection of Persons in the Event of Maritime Accidents
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on the application of the Convention on Protection of People in the Case of Maritime Incidents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The decision and the regulation aim to ensure that EU countries’ laws and regulations are compatible with the Athens convention on maritime accidents.
The regulation sets out the obligations of EU countries and the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA) to ensure a high level of compensation for passengers involved in maritime accidents, and the rules for insurance companies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The convention applies to commercial carriage undertaken by EU countries or public authorities under contract of carriage within the territory of an EU country.
It does not apply to bareboat charter-out registration, in which the flag State for the purposes of bareboat registration is either a Member State or a contracting party to the convention.
EU countries must apply the convention’s rules for liability for damage caused to passengers and for loss of or damage to luggage and vehicles during the carriage of passengers and their cabin luggage during the period covered by the convention (i.e.
the period during which the passenger and his cabin luggage are on board the ship or in the course of embarkation or disembarkation, or the period of transport by water from land to the ship and vice-versa, if the cost of such transport is included in the fare or if the vessel used for this purpose of auxiliary transport has been put at the disposal of the passenger by the carrier.
Compulsory insurance and limitation of liability of insurers
Insurance companies must provide passengers with compulsory financial security for the duration of the carriage.
Insurers must provide the passenger with:
a certificate in the official language of the issuing EU country;
a statement of the identity of the carrier;
the name and address of the insurer;
an identification number of the passengers;
information on the type of baggage and the number of vehicles involved in the incident.
A certificate must be issued by the issuing country in accordance with the rules of the convention and must contain the following information:
the identity and nationality of the claimant and the date and place of death or injury;
details of the loss or damage caused by the incident and the amount of the compensation to be paid.
An insurance company must also provide the claimant with information on the number and type of the baggage and vehicles involved, including the type and quantity of the insurance policy.
Liability of carriers
The carrier is liable for the loss suffered as a result of the incident which caused the loss if the incident was due to the fault or neglect of a carrier. The burden of proving fault or negligence lies with the claimant. The carrier is also liable if the loss was caused by negligence on the part of a passenger who knew or ought reasonably to have known of the injury, loss and damage caused.
Limitations of liability
The liability of carriers is limited to 250,000 units of account per passenger on each distinct occasion. Insurance companies must ensure that the liability limit is not less than €21,000 for each passenger.
If a passenger dies or is seriously injured, the compensation must not be less than EUR 21 000. If the death or the injury or damage is caused by a vehicle, the claimant must be entitled to compensation of at least €20,000.
Carriers must notify the claimant of any damage to their baggage and/or vehicles.
Notice of loss of luggage and damage to vehicles
The claimant must give the carrier at least 30 days’ written notice of any loss of their baggage or vehicles. The notice must be provided in the most appropriate format.
Disputes must be settled by the claimant within 30 days of the date on which the incident took place. The claimant must provide proof that the carrier knew or should have known that their luggage or vehicles were damaged or that the incident caused damage.
Amendments to the regulation
Amending Regulation (EU) 2018/852 amends the regulation to take account of the fact that the convention has been amended by the International Monetary Fund (IMO) and that the EU is not a party to it.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS AND REGULATIONS APPLY?
Directive 1999/469/EC has applied since 1 January 1999 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2000.
Regulations (EC, EC, EC) No 550/2004 have applied since 31 December 2004.

Summary 143:
European rail network for competitive freight made up of rail freight corridors — Regulation (EU) 2018/2021
European rail system for competitive and efficient freight — Regulation(EU) 2020/2031
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 2021/2010 on the establishment of a European rail network of freight corridors
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the establishment and management of freight rail corridors in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all rail freight routes in the EU.
It does not apply to intermodal freight terminals, such as those in the Baltic Sea, the Black Sea or the Mediterranean Sea, which are not part of the EU’s internal rail market.
The objectives of the regulation are to:
establish the European rail freight market;
provide a single market for rail freight across the EU;
facilitate investment in rail freight infrastructure;
ensure that rail freight traffic is managed in a way that meets, subject to economic viability, the needs of the freight corridor.
Decision-making process
The European Commission is responsible for the decision-making procedure for establishing a rail freight corridor and for the management of the rail freight network. It must inform the competent infrastructure managers of these applications and the decision taken without delay.
A management board composed of representatives from the EU countries and the infrastructure managers must draw up, regularly update and publish a document containing:
all the information contained in the network statement for national rail networks regarding the freight corridors;
the information on the rail infrastructure is available to the management board;
a report on the progress of the development of rail infrastructure in the corridor and on the use of the corridor;
information on the cost-effectiveness of investment in the rail corridor is available on the one-stop shop for further processing.
An advisory group composed of railway undertakings is set up to advise the European Commission on the implementation of this regulation. It may also issue own-initiative opinions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2010.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU has been working towards establishing a single European rail market for freight for more than 20 years.
For more information, see:
‘Rail freight’ on the Euratom website.
* KEY TERMS
* Freight rail network: a network of rail tracks which are used by rail freight trains to transport goods and passengers between different EU countries.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Euratom), Euratom, of 29 July 2010 on the establishing of a rail network in the Union for competitive, efficient and environmentally sound freight made of rail corridors

Summary 144:
Road transport operators — common rules for the qualification of road transport operators
Road transportation operators — Common rules for their qualification
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2011 on common rules on the qualification and monitoring of the occupational qualifications for road transport operator
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the quality of the European Union’s (EU) road transport sector by harmonising the qualification requirements for the occupational qualification of the professionals involved in road transport operations.
It also aims to reduce administrative burdens for EU countries and improve the safety of road users.
KEY POINTS
 
The regulation applies to all undertakings which intend to engage in the occupation of road transportation operator. It does not apply to undertakings operating in several EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that undertakings have an effective and stable establishment.
To be admitted to the occupation, an undertaking must meet certain conditions, including:
a minimum level of financial standing;
a certificate of professional competence and good repute;
the transport manager must have a certificate issued by a competent judicial or administrative authority in the EU country in which the transport manager or any other relevant person used to reside.
An EU country may exempt from the examinations persons who can provide proof of continuous experience in managing transport activities.
Exemption from examination
Exemptions may be granted to persons who have been declared unfit by an EU country for a number of reasons, such as:
being a national of another EU country;
being an employee of a non-EU country; or
country of nationality other than that of the EU.
Certificates relating to financial standing must be produced as proof of their financial standing.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 4 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
Directive 2011/22/EU on the European Commission's Directorate-General for Employment, Social Affairs and Inclusion of the Member States in the European Economic Area (EEA) (see summary)
KEY TERMS
* Road transport operator: undertakings engaged in road passenger transport services exclusively for non-commercial purposes or which have a main occupation other than the main occupation in the field of road traffic management.
* Non-commercial: any company engaged in the transport of goods or services by means of motor vehicles with a maximum authorised speed not exceeding 40 km/h.
